Rulings on Coins with Predominant Impurity
Expansion of the ruling on impure coins with cautionary fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on coins with predominant impurity (ghalib al-ghishsh) and determine the permissibility of selling them with disparity, based on the distinction between pure and impure coins.

Core Idea
Coins with predominant impurity are like copper coins; disparity is permitted in principle but cautioned to prevent riba.

In the rules of currency exchange (sarf), the Hanafi school distinguishes between pure silver/gold coins and coins where the base metal predominates. This lesson explores the legal status of such coins—called ghalib al-ghishsh—and the practical implications for buying, selling, and borrowing. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq, highlighting both the basic permissibility of disparity and the caution exercised by later scholars to prevent opening the door to riba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coin category
Determine whether a coin is pure dirham/dinar or is ghalib al-ghishsh (impurity predominant). The key is the proportion of impurity: if base metal exceeds silver/gold, it falls under this category.
The text refers to coins like al-Adali and al-Ghatarifa as examples.
2
State the basic ruling
For ghalib al-ghishsh, they are not treated as pure currency; thus selling them for their own kind with disparity (تفاضل) is valid (sahih) according to the literal ruling (zahir al-riwayah).
This contrasts with pure dirhams/dinars, where disparity is prohibited due to riba.
3
Understand the caution of the Mashayikh
Despite the literal permissibility, scholars of Transoxiana refrained from giving fatwa on disparity for certain coins (al-Adali and al-Ghatarifa) to prevent riba, as people would gradually transition to pure coins.
This shows the role of sadd al-dhara'i (blocking means to evil).
4
Apply custom (urf)
The method of transacting these coins—whether by weight or count—follows local custom, as they are akin to copper coins (fulus). Also, they are not specified by designation (ta'yin) as long as they circulate.
Custom overrides default rules when no explicit text exists.
5
Differentiate between circulating and non-circulating coins
If the coins are no longer in use, they revert to being a commodity and become specified by designation. This affects nullification of the contract if they perish before delivery.
The passage links ta'yin to the continued existence of thamaniyyah (price-ness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصرف عدداً أو بيع بعضها ببعض إلا متساوياً وزناً ولا يصح الاستقراض بهما إلا وزناً وغالب الغش ليس في حكم الدراهم والدنانير فيصح بيعها بجنسها متفاضلاً والتبايع والاستقراض بما يروج عدداً أو وزناً أو بهما ولا يتعين بالتعيين لكونها أثمانا وتتعين بالتعين إن كانت لا تروج والمتساوي يعتبران أصلاً، ولا يجوز بيعه بجنسه متفاضلاً إن كان موزوناً للربا. وفي الهداية : ومشايخنا - يعني مشايخ ما وراء النهر من بخارى وسمرقند - لم يفتوا بجواز ذلك أي ببيعها بجنسها متفاضلاً في العدالي والغطارفة مع أن الغش فيها أكثر من الفضة لأنها أعز الأموال في ديارنا، فلو أبيح التفاضل فيها ينفتح باب الربا الصريح فإن الناس حينئذ يعتادون في الأموال النفيسة فيتدرجون ذلك في النقود الخالصة والغطارفة دراهم منسوبة إلى غطريف - بكسر الغين المعجمة وسكون الطاء وكسر الراء بعدها الياء وآخرها الفاء - ابن عطاء الكندي أمير خراسان أيام الرشيد، وقيل هو خال الرشيد والعدالي بفتح العين المهملة وتخفيف الدال المهملة وباللام المكسورة وهي الدراهم المنسوبة إلى العدال وكأنه اسم ملك نسب إليه درهم فيه غش؛ كذا في البناية والغش بمعنى المغشوش وهو غير الخالص ؛ كذا في القاموس قوله : (والتبايع والاستقراض بما يروج عدداً أو وزناً أو بهما لأن المعتبر فيما لا نص فيه العادة لأنها صارت بغلبة الغش كالفلوس فيعتبر فيها العادة كالفلوس فإن كانت تروج بالوزن فيه وبالعد فيه وبهما فبكل منهما قوله : (ولا يتعين بالتعيين لكونها أثماناً) لأنها ما دامت تروج يعني موجوداً لا تبطل الثمنية لقيام المقتضى. أثماناً فما دام ذلك الاصطلاح بالاصطلاح صارت قوله : ( وتتعين بالتعين إن كانت لا تروج لزوال المقتضى للثمنية وهو الاصطلاح، وهذا لأنها في الأصل سلعة وإنما صارت أثماناً بالاصطلاح فإذا تركوا المعاملة بها رجعت إلى أصلها، وإن كان يأخذها البعض فهي مثل الدراهم لا يتعلق العقد بعينها بل بجنسها إن كان البائع يعلم بحالها، وإن كان لا يعلم بحالها وباعه بها على ظن أنها دراهم جياد تعلق حقه بالجياد لوجود الرضا بها في الأول وعدمه في الثاني. وأشار بالتعيين عند عدم رواجها وبعدمه عند رواجها إلى أنها إذا هلكت قبل القبض لا يبطل العقد إن كانت رائجة، ويبطل إن لم تكن. وأطلق في تعيينها وهو مقيد بما إذا كانا يعلمان بحالها ويعلم كل من المتعاقدين أن عبارة الزيلعي : والفرق بينهما أن الفضة المغلوبة أو الذهب المغلوب موجود حقيقة من حيث اللون ومآلاً بالإذابة، فإن الفضة أو الذهب يخلصان منه بالإذابة فكانا موجودين حقيقة وحكماً حتى يعتبر ما فيه من الفضة والذهب من النصاب في الزكاة أيضاً بخلاف الغش المغلوب بهما لأنه يحترق ويهلك ولا لون له في الحال أيضاً الخ. وهو أفهم المقصود ما هنا
The Book of Currency Exchange. By number, or selling some of them with others except equal in weight, and borrowing with them is not valid except by weight. And coin with predominant impurity is not in the ruling of dirhams and dinars, so selling it for its own kind with disparity is valid. And transactions and borrowing are according to what circulates, by number or weight or both. And it is not specified by designation because it is a price, and it becomes specified by designation if it does not circulate. And the equal (mix) is considered as original, and selling it for its own kind with disparity is not permissible if it is weighed, because of riba. And in al-Hidayah: Our Mashayikh – meaning the Mashayikh of Transoxiana from Bukhara and Samarkand – did not give fatwa on permissibility of that, i.e., selling them for their own kind with disparity in al-Adali and al-Ghatarifa, even though the impurity is more than silver, because they are the most valuable wealth in our lands. If disparity were permitted, the door to clear riba would open, as people would then accustom themselves to valuable wealth and gradually move to pure coins... [continues]
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, explaining the legal status of coins where the impurity (base metal) predominates over silver or gold. The author first states that such coins are not treated as pure dirhams or dinars; therefore, selling them for their own kind with disparity (تفاضل) is valid. However, he also reports from al-Hidayah that the scholars of Transoxiana refrained from issuing fatwa on this permissibility in the case of specific coins (al-Adali and al-Ghatarifa), even though they contain more impurity than silver, to prevent opening the door to riba, as people would become accustomed to disparity in valuable items and gradually transition to pure currencies. The passage then discusses the rules for transaction: these coins are transacted according to custom (عادة) – whether by weight or count – because they are akin to copper coins (فلوس). They do not become specified by designation (تعيين) as long as they circulate, because they retain the quality of being a price (ثمنية). If they cease to circulate, they revert to being a commodity and become specified. The author also cites al-Zayla'i's distinction: when silver or gold is predominant, it is considered existing in reality and effect, whereas when impurity is predominant, it burns and vanishes; thus, the ruling differs. The passage concludes that this is the intended understanding.

Key Terms
غالب الغش (ghālib al-ghishsh): Coins in which the impurity (base metal) predominates over silver/gold content.
التعيين (al-ta'yīn): Specification of particular coins in a contract by pointing or designation.
المتساوي (al-mutasāwī): A coin where silver and impurity are equal in quantity (half and half).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the operative (mufta bihi) position regarding selling coins with predominant impurity (e.g., al-Adali) for their own kind with disparity?
A) It is absolutely permissible.
B) It is absolutely impermissible.
C) It is permissible in principle but the later scholars advised against it to prevent riba.
D) It is only permissible if both parties are merchants.

Key Takeaway
Coins with predominant impurity are not treated as pure currency; disparity is permitted in the literal ruling, but the mufta bihi is to avoid it to prevent sliding into riba.

The Dispute: Does Currency Obsolescence Void a Sale?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale validity after currency obsolescence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Imam Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn) on the effect of currency obsolescence on a sale and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa voids the sale; Sahibayn hold it continues with payment of value.

When a currency ceases to circulate before the buyer pays, does the sale collapse? Imam Abu Hanifa says yes—the contract becomes impossible to perform. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing the impossibility might be temporary. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The buyer purchases something with current dirhams or fulus (copper coins). Before handing them over, the coins become obsolete (kasād) or are withdrawn from circulation.
The currency had rawāj (circulation) at the time of the contract.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's View and Reasoning
Imam says the sale becomes void (batal) because delivery of the specific currency is impossible; the custom (ʿurf) that made them price is gone.
The buyer must return the item if existent, or its value if destroyed.
3
State the Sahibayn's View and Reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the sale does not become void; obsolescence may be temporary. The buyer must pay the value (qīma) of the currency.
They differ on valuation date: Abu Yusuf – day of contract, Muhammad – day of obsolescence.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical difference: Under Imam, the seller gets back the item. Under Sahibayn, the seller receives monetary compensation instead.
The ruling affects who bears the risk of currency fluctuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو اشترى بها أو بفلوس نافقة شيئاً وكسدت بطل البيع) أي اشترى بالدراهم التي غلب عليها الغش أو بالفلوس وكان كل منهما نافقاً . جاز البيع لقيام الاصطلاح على الثمنية، ولعدم الحاجة إلى الإشارة لالتحاقها بالثمن ولم يسلمها المشتري إلى البائع ثم كسدت بطل البيع والانقطاع عن أيدي الناس كالكساد وحكم كذلك، فإن اشترى بالدراهم ثم كسدت أو انقطعت بطل البيع، ويجب على المشتري رد المبيع إن كان قائماً، ومثله إن كان هالكاً وكان مثلياً وإلا فقيمته، وإن لم يكن مقبوضاً فلا حكم لهذا البيع أصلاً، وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : لا يبطل البيع لأنه المتعذر إنما هو التسليم بعد الكساد وذلك لا يوجب الفساد لاحتمال الزوال بالرواج كما لو اشترى شيئاً بالرطب ثم انقطع وإذا لم يبطل وتعذر تسليمه وجبت قيمته لكن عند أبي يوسف يوم البيع، وعند محمد الكساد وهو آخر ما يتعامل الناس بها. وفي الذخيرة الفتوى على قول أبو يوسف. وفي الدراهم يوم يوم. حتى إنما يبطل العقد إذا اختار المشتري إبطاله فسخاً لأن كسادها بمنزلة عيب فيها والأول أظهر قوله : والانقطاع عن أيدي الناس كالكساد قال الرملي : الحق هذا الشارح الانقطاع بالكساد تبعاً للزيلعي . وفي المضمرات قال : فإن انقطع ذلك فعليه من الذهب والفضة قيمته في آخر يوم انقطع هو المختار .
His saying: (If one buys something with them or with current fulus, and then they become obsolete, the sale becomes void.) Meaning: he bought with dirhams that have predominant impurity or with fulus, and both were current. The sale is valid due to the custom establishing them as prices, and no need for specification because they are like price. Then if the buyer did not hand them over to the seller and they became obsolete, the sale becomes void. And removal from the hands of people is like obsolescence, and the ruling is the same. If he bought with dirhams and then they became obsolete or were removed, the sale becomes void, and the buyer must return the sold item if it exists, or its equivalent if it is fungible and perishable, or its value otherwise. And if it is not yet received, there is no ruling for this sale at all. This is according to Imam. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The sale does not become void because what becomes impossible is only delivery after obsolescence, and that does not necessitate invalidity due to the possibility of revival by circulation, just as if one bought something with fresh dates and then they ceased. If it does not become void and delivery becomes impossible, its value must be paid: according to Abu Yusuf, the value on the day of sale; according to Muhammad, the value at the time of obsolescence, which is the last day people transact with them. In al-Dhakhira, the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf. And in al-Mudmarat: If that ceases, then he must pay in gold and silver its value on the last day it ceased; that is the chosen view.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of currency obsolescence (كساد) on a sale. The scenario: a buyer purchases something using dirhams with predominant impurity or copper coins (fulus) that are currently circulating (نافقة). If, before the buyer delivers the payment to the seller, these coins cease to circulate (become obsolete or are withdrawn from public use), the ruling differs. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the sale becomes void (بطل البيع) because the specific currency is no longer acceptable as payment, making performance impossible. The buyer must return the purchased item if it still exists, or its equivalent or value if it perished. If the item has not been delivered, the sale is completely invalid. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the sale does not become void because the impossibility may be temporary – the coins could become current again. Instead, the buyer must pay the value (قيمة) of the currency. Abu Yusuf assesses the value at the time of the contract, while Muhammad assesses it at the time of obsolescence (the last day the coins were traded). The text from al-Dhakhira states the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view, but al-Mudmarat prefers the view that the buyer pays the value on the last day of circulation. The commentator also clarifies that 'cessation of circulation' is equivalent to 'obsolescence' in this ruling.

Key Terms
كساد (kasād): Obsolescence or cessation of circulation of currency; it becomes no longer accepted in transactions.
رواج (rawāj): The circulation and acceptance of currency in the market.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract; here, the buyer may choose to rescind due to obsolescence being a defect.
نافقة (nāfiqa): Currency that is current and accepted in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute regarding the effect of currency becoming obsolete (كساد) on a sale, what is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The sale is valid and the buyer must pay the value.
B) The sale is void (batal).
C) The sale is suspended (mawquf).
D) The sale is fasid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when currency becomes obsolete before delivery, the mufta bihi view is that the sale remains valid and the buyer must pay the value (at the time of contract per Abu Yusuf).

Determining the Value: Date of Contract or Date of Obsolescence?
Intra-Sahibayn dispute on valuation date after currency obsolescence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's positions on the valuation date and identify the mufta bihi according to later sources.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf values at contract date; Muhammad values at obsolescence date; fatwa may differ.

Even within the Sahibayn's view that the sale does not void, there is a further dispute: at what point should the value of the obsolete currency be assessed? Abu Yusuf says the day of sale; Muhammad says the last day of circulation. This lesson examines the reasoning and the tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Sahibayn's Position
The Sahibayn agree that the sale remains valid and the buyer must pay the value (qīma) of the currency. They differ on the date of valuation.
This sub-dispute only matters if the sale is not voided.
2
State Abu Yusuf's View and Basis
Abu Yusuf says value is assessed at the time of the contract (yawm al-bayʿ). The basis is that the obligation to pay arose at that moment.
This protects the seller against post-contract depreciation.
3
State Muhammad's View and Basis
Muhammad says value is assessed at the time of obsolescence (yawm al-kasād), the last day the currency was traded. The basis is that the true loss occurs when the currency becomes useless.
This may better reflect the actual harm to the seller.
4
Examine the Tarjih
Al-Dhakhira gives fatwa on Abu Yusuf's view. Al-Mudmarat prefers Muhammad's view as the chosen (al-mukhtār) position. The commentator notes that the earlier view (al-awwal) is more apparent (aẓhar) but does not specify final tarjih.
The tarjih may depend on the context and later custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو اشترى بها أو بفلوس نافقة شيئاً وكسدت بطل البيع) أي اشترى بالدراهم التي غلب عليها الغش أو بالفلوس وكان كل منهما نافقاً . جاز البيع لقيام الاصطلاح على الثمنية، ولعدم الحاجة إلى الإشارة لالتحاقها بالثمن ولم يسلمها المشتري إلى البائع ثم كسدت بطل البيع والانقطاع عن أيدي الناس كالكساد وحكم كذلك، فإن اشترى بالدراهم ثم كسدت أو انقطعت بطل البيع، ويجب على المشتري رد المبيع إن كان قائماً، ومثله إن كان هالكاً وكان مثلياً وإلا فقيمته، وإن لم يكن مقبوضاً فلا حكم لهذا البيع أصلاً، وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : لا يبطل البيع لأنه المتعذر إنما هو التسليم بعد الكساد وذلك لا يوجب الفساد لاحتمال الزوال بالرواج كما لو اشترى شيئاً بالرطب ثم انقطع وإذا لم يبطل وتعذر تسليمه وجبت قيمته لكن عند أبي يوسف يوم البيع، وعند محمد الكساد وهو آخر ما يتعامل الناس بها. وفي الذخيرة الفتوى على قول أبو يوسف. وفي الدراهم يوم يوم. حتى إنما يبطل العقد إذا اختار المشتري إبطاله فسخاً لأن كسادها بمنزلة عيب فيها والأول أظهر قوله : والانقطاع عن أيدي الناس كالكساد قال الرملي : الحق هذا الشارح الانقطاع بالكساد تبعاً للزيلعي . وفي المضمرات قال : فإن انقطع ذلك فعليه من الذهب والفضة قيمته في آخر يوم انقطع هو المختار .
His saying: (If one buys something with them or with current fulus, and then they become obsolete, the sale becomes void.) Meaning: he bought with dirhams that have predominant impurity or with fulus, and both were current. The sale is valid due to the custom establishing them as prices, and no need for specification because they are like price. Then if the buyer did not hand them over to the seller and they became obsolete, the sale becomes void. And removal from the hands of people is like obsolescence, and the ruling is the same. If he bought with dirhams and then they became obsolete or were removed, the sale becomes void, and the buyer must return the sold item if it exists, or its equivalent if it is fungible and perishable, or its value otherwise. And if it is not yet received, there is no ruling for this sale at all. This is according to Imam. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The sale does not become void because what becomes impossible is only delivery after obsolescence, and that does not necessitate invalidity due to the possibility of revival by circulation, just as if one bought something with fresh dates and then they ceased. If it does not become void and delivery becomes impossible, its value must be paid: according to Abu Yusuf, the value on the day of sale; according to Muhammad, the value at the time of obsolescence, which is the last day people transact with them. In al-Dhakhira, the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf. And in al-Mudmarat: If that ceases, then he must pay in gold and silver its value on the last day it ceased; that is the chosen view.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of currency obsolescence (كساد) on a sale. The scenario: a buyer purchases something using dirhams with predominant impurity or copper coins (fulus) that are currently circulating (نافقة). If, before the buyer delivers the payment to the seller, these coins cease to circulate (become obsolete or are withdrawn from public use), the ruling differs. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the sale becomes void (بطل البيع) because the specific currency is no longer acceptable as payment, making performance impossible. The buyer must return the purchased item if it still exists, or its equivalent or value if it perished. If the item has not been delivered, the sale is completely invalid. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the sale does not become void because the impossibility may be temporary – the coins could become current again. Instead, the buyer must pay the value (قيمة) of the currency. Abu Yusuf assesses the value at the time of the contract, while Muhammad assesses it at the time of obsolescence (the last day the coins were traded). The text from al-Dhakhira states the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view, but al-Mudmarat prefers the view that the buyer pays the value on the last day of circulation. The commentator also clarifies that 'cessation of circulation' is equivalent to 'obsolescence' in this ruling.

Key Terms
قيمة (qīma): Value; monetary compensation for an item when the original currency is no longer acceptable.
كساد (kasād): Obsolescence or cessation of circulation of currency; it becomes no longer accepted in transactions.
رواج (rawāj): The circulation and acceptance of currency in the market.
المختار (al-mukhtār): The chosen/preferred view; in al-Mudmarat, it refers to Muhammad's position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Dhakhira, which imam's view on the valuation date is given as the fatwa?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Neither; it is left undetermined.

Key Takeaway
When currency becomes obsolete in a sale, the buyer must pay its value; the mufta bihi per al-Dhakhira is value at contract date (Abu Yusuf), but al-Mudmarat prefers value at obsolescence.

Ruling on Sale with Defunct Coins: Knowledge and Currency Acceptance
Ruling on sale with defunct coins: conditions of knowledge and acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale when the payment coins become defunct, and identify the conditions that determine whether the sale is tied to the specific coins or to the common currency.

Core Idea
A sale with defunct coins is not automatically void—it depends on the knowledge of the parties and local acceptance.

When coins lose their value or acceptance, does the sale collapse? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer: the ruling hinges on whether the parties knew the coins were defunct and on the level of acceptance in the market. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the conditions that save or void the transaction, emphasizing fairness and common practice.

Guided Path
1
Classify the coins
Determine whether the coins are predominantly silver or base metal, and whether they are current (nāfiq) or defunct (kāsad). The status affects the ruling.
The text uses 'كغالب الفضة' as an analogy for the predominant metal.
2
Assess the knowledge condition
Identify if both parties know the coins are defunct, or if only one knows, or if neither knows. Knowledge is key to validity.
If neither knows, or one does not know, the sale is tied to the common currency.
3
Apply the default rule for ignorance
If both or one party is ignorant of the defunct status, the sale is not void but instead tied to the current common currency (al-ra'ij) of the land.
This preserves the contract rather than voiding it.
4
Handle partial acceptance
If some people accept the coins and others reject them, treat them like counterfeit (ziyūf) or adulterated (nabharja). The sale is tied to the genus of the coin, not the specific coins.
The seller must know the specific condition of the coins for this to apply.
5
Determine the default when seller does not know
If the seller does not know the condition, the contract is tied to the most commonly accepted coin. If acceptance is equal, refer to earlier details from the beginning of the book of sale.
This ensures a fair default for both parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كغالب الفضة في التبايع والاستقراض وفي الصرف كغالب الغش ولو اشترى بها أو بفلوس نافقة شيئاً وكسدت بطل البيع وصح البيع بالفلوس النافقة وإن لم تتعين وبالكاسدة لا حتى الآخر يعلم، فإن كانا لا يعلمان أو لا يعلم أحدهما أو يعلمان ولا يعلم كل أن الآخر يعلم فإن البيع يتعلق بالدراهم الرائجة في ذلك البلد لا بالمشار إليه من هذه الدراهم التي لا تروج، وإن كان يقبلها البعض ويردها البعض فهي في حكم الزيوف والنبهرجة فيتعلق البيع بجنسها لا بعينها كما هو في المرابحة، لكن يشترط أن يعلم البائع خاصة ذلك أمرها لأنه من رضي بذلك وأدرج نفسه في البعض الذين يقبلونها. وإن كان البائع لا يعلم تعلق العقد على الأروج، فإن استوت في الرواج جرى التفصيل الذي أسلفناه في أول كتاب البيع؛ كذا في فتح القدير
Like predominantly silver in sale and loan, and in exchange like predominantly adulterated. If one buys with them or with current copper coins something and they become defunct, the sale is void. Sale with current copper coins is valid even if not specified, and with defunct ones not unless the other knows. If both do not know, or one does not know, or they know but each does not know that the other knows, then the sale is tied to the current coins of that land, not to the specific indicated coins that are not current. If some accept them and some reject them, they are in the ruling of counterfeit and adulterated, so the sale is tied to their genus, not to the specific, as in deferred payment sale, but it is a condition that the seller knows the particular condition, because he consented to that and placed himself among those who accept them. If the seller does not know, the contract is tied to the most current. If they are equal in currency, the details we set forth at the beginning of the book of sale apply. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn statement regarding coins that are predominantly silver or predominantly adulterated. It explains the conditions for a sale when the coins used are defunct (kasād) or partially accepted. The key point is that the validity of the sale depends on the knowledge of the parties about the coins' status and whether the coins are locally accepted as currency. If both parties are ignorant or one is ignorant, the sale is tied to the common currency of the land (al-ra'ij) rather than the specific defunct coins. If some people accept the coins and others reject them, they are treated like counterfeit (ziyūf) and adulterated (nabharja), and the sale is tied to the genus, not the specific coins, provided the seller knows their particular condition. If the seller does not know, the contract is tied to the most widely accepted currency. If the currency is equally accepted, the earlier details from the beginning of the book of sale apply. This ruling is cited from Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
كساد (kasād): The state of coins being no longer accepted in transactions, i.e., defunct or out of circulation.
نافق (nāfiq): Coins that are current and accepted in transactions (literally 'passing').
رائج (rā’ij): The most common and widely accepted currency in a particular locality.
زيوف (ziyūf): Counterfeit coins that are rejected by some people.
نبهرجة (nabharaja): Adulterated coins of low quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the buyer pays with coins that are defunct and the seller does not know, on what basis is the contract tied?
A) The specific coins indicated
B) The most common currency (al-arwaj)
C) The buyer's choice of coins
D) The contract is void

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school preserves the validity of a sale with defunct coins by tying it to the common currency, provided the seller knows the coin's condition or accepts the local default.

Equal-Mix Coins: Sale, Loan, and Exchange (Sarf) in Hanafi Fiqh
Explaining the legal treatment of equal-mix coins in different contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on equal-mix coins in sale, loan, and sarf, and explain the reason for the different treatment in sarf.

Core Idea
Equal-mix coins: treated as silver in sale/loan (by weight), as adulterated in sarf (require more pure silver) to avoid riba.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq clarifies the Hanafi position on coins made of equal parts silver and base metal. In ordinary sale and loan, they are treated like silver coins and must be transacted by weight. In currency exchange (ṣarf), however, they are treated like predominantly adulterated coins, requiring that if exchanged for pure silver, the pure silver must outweigh the silver content in the mixed coins. This ruling is based on the principle that when silver is not predominant, caution is applied to prevent riba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Coin Type
Recognize mutasāwī as coins with equal silver and adulteration, neither metal predominant.
2
Distinguish Transaction Types
Separate sale/loan (bayʿ/qarḍ) from exchange (ṣarf) because each has different rules.
3
Apply Ruling for Sale/Loan
They must be sold or lent by weight, not by count, because silver exists and is not overwhelmed.
Like inferior silver coins.
4
Apply Ruling for Sarf
If exchanging for pure silver, the pure silver must be more in weight than the silver content. If exchanging for the same genus (e.g., another equal-mix coin), equality of weight is required as a precaution.
The mixed coin is treated as all silver in sarf to avoid riba.
5
Understand the Rationale
Since neither metal dominates, caution mandates treating the coin as all silver in sarf, leading to the superiority requirement for different genera and equality for same genus.
The apparent contradiction with al-Khaniyya is reconciled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمتساوي كغالب الفضة في التبايع والاستقراض وفي الصرف كغالب الغش) يعني فلا يجوز البيع بها ولا إقراضها إلا بالوزن بمنزلة الدراهم الرديئة لأن الفضة موجودة فيها حقيقة ولم تصر مغلوبة فيجب الاعتبار بالوزن شرعاً. وإذا أشار إليها في المبايعة كان بياناً لقدرها ،ووصفها ولا يبطل البيع بهلاكها قبل القبض ويعطيه مثلها لكونها ثمناً لم تتعين، وأما في الصرف فيجب بيعها بجنسها على وجه الاعتبار ولو باعها بالفضة الخالصة لم يجز حتى يكون الخالص أكثر مما فيه الفضة لأنه لا غلبة لأحدهما على الآخر فيجب اعتبارهما . وفي الخانية : إن كان نصفها صفراً ونصفها فضة لا يجوز التفاضل فظاهره أنه أراد به فيما إذا بيعت بجنسها وهو مخالف لما ذكر هنا ووجهه أن فضتها لما لم تصر مغلوبة جعلت كان كلها فضة في حق الصرف احتياطاً
His statement: 'And the equal [coins] are like predominantly silver in sale and loan, and in exchange like predominantly adulterated.' Meaning: it is not permissible to sell them or lend them except by weight, like inferior coins, because silver exists in them truly and has not become overwhelmed, so consideration by weight is obligatory legally. If he points to them in sale, it is a statement of their amount and description, and the sale is not invalidated by their destruction before delivery, and he gives their like because they are price (thaman) not specified. As for exchange (sarf), it is obligatory to sell them by their genus with consideration, and if he sells them with pure silver, it is not permissible unless the pure is more than what is in them of silver, because neither overwhelms the other, so both must be considered. And in al-Khaniyya: If half is copper and half silver, disparity is not allowed. Its apparent meaning is that he meant when sold by its genus, and it contradicts what is mentioned here. The reason is that since the silver has not become overwhelmed, it is considered as all silver in respect of exchange as a precaution.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage comments on a matn statement: 'And the equal [coins]' meaning coins with equal parts silver and adulteration. The ruling: In sale and loan, these coins are treated like predominantly silver (i.e., must be transacted by weight) because the silver is genuinely present and not overwhelmed. In exchange (sarf), they are treated like predominantly adulterated, meaning that if exchanged for silver, the silver must be more in quantity to avoid riba. The text then cites al-Khaniyya, which says that if half copper and half silver, no disparity is allowed in exchange (i.e., must be equal weight). This seems contradictory but is reconciled by the explanation that since the silver is not overwhelmed, it is treated as all silver for caution in sarf, thus requiring equality. So the ruling is consistent: in sarf, to avoid riba, the pure silver must be more than the silver content, or if sold for its own genus, it must be equal weight.

Key Terms
متساوي (mutasāwī): Coins with equal parts silver and adulteration (e.g., half copper, half silver).
صرف (ṣarf): Currency exchange, specifically the exchange of gold/silver for gold/silver, subject to strict rules to avoid riba.
غلبة (ghalaba): Predominance; if silver is predominant, the coin is treated as silver; if adulteration is predominant, the coin is treated as a commodity.
احتياط (iḥtiyāt): Precautionary measure; here, treating equal-mix coins as all silver in sarf to ensure no riba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when exchanging equal-mix coins (half silver, half copper) for pure silver, what condition must be met?
A) The weights must be equal
B) The pure silver must be more in weight than the silver content of the mixed coins
C) The pure silver must be less in weight
D) Such an exchange is not allowed at all

Key Takeaway
Equal-mix coins require weight-based sale/loan and, in sarf, either the pure metal must outweigh the silver or equality is mandatory to prevent riba.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Does a Sale Void if Currency Becomes Defunct Before Delivery?
Explaining the intra-Hanafi dispute on currency defunctness before delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the effect of defunct currency before delivery, identify the mufta bihi, and explain the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
The mufta bihi: sale remains valid; buyer pays value on day of sale.

The matn states that a sale becomes void if the currency used becomes defunct before delivery. However, a closer look reveals a rich intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa held the sale void due to loss of thamanīya, while his two disciples argued it remains valid because the currency might return to circulation. This lesson unpacks the arguments, defines key terms, and identifies the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Matn's Ruling
Identify the initial ruling in the text: 'if one buys ... and they become defunct, the sale is void.' This is the starting point.
The matn does not mention the dispute explicitly.
2
Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's Position and Dalil
The commentator explains that according to Imam, the sale is void because the attribute of being currency (thamanīya) is lost, and the contract was based on that attribute.
Imam's dalil: the ruling is like buying with something that ceases to be valid.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's Position and Dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue the sale is not void because the impossibility of delivery is temporary; the coins might become current again, like fresh dates going out of season.
They hold the sale valid but with compensation.
4
Note the Further Dispute on Valuation
Even among the Sahibayn, there is disagreement: Abu Yusuf says the buyer pays the value on the day of sale, Muhammad says on the day of defunctness.
This practical difference affects the amount owed.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Tarjih Authority
The text states that in al-Dhakhira, the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view: sale valid, value on day of sale. Some later scholars prefer Muhammad's view for ease.
Thus the operative ruling for fatwa is the sale is valid with compensation on the day of sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو اشترى بها أو بفلوس نافقة شيئاً وكسدت بطل البيع) أي اشترى بالدراهم التي غلب عليها الغش أو بالفلوس وكان كل منهما نافقاً . جاز البيع لقيام لاصطلاح على الثمنية، ولعدم الحاجة إلى الإشارة لالتحاقها بالثمن ولم يسلمها المشتري إلى البائع ثم كسدت بطل البيع والانقطاع عن أيدي الناس كالكساد وحكم كذلك، فإن اشترى بالدراهم ثم كسدت أو انقطعت بطل البيع، ويجب على المشتري رد المبيع إن كان قائماً، ومثله إن كان هالكاً وكان مثلياً وإلا فقيمته، وإن لم يكن مقبوضاً فلا حكم لهذا البيع أصلاً، وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : لا يبطل البيع لأنه المتعذر إنما هو التسليم بعد الكساد وذلك لا يوجب الفساد لاحتمال الزوال بالرواج كما لو اشترى شيئاً بالرطب ثم انقطع وإذا لم يبطل وتعذر تسليمه وجبت قيمته لكن عند أبي يوسف يوم البيع، وعند محمد الكساد وهو آخر ما يتعامل الناس بها. وفي الذخيرة الفتوى على قول أبو يوسف. وفي الدراهم يوم . حتى إنما يبطل العقد إذا اختار المشتري إبطاله فسخاً لأن كسادها بمنزلة عيب فيها والأول أظهر قوله : والانقطاع عن أيدي الناس كالكساد قال الرملي : الحق هذا الشارح الانقطاع بالكساد تبعاً للزيلعي . وفي المضمرات قال : فإن انقطع ذلك فعليه من الذهب والفضة قيمته في آخر يوم انقطع هو المختار . المحيط والتتمة والحقائق : بقول محمد يفتي رفقاً بالناس ولأبي حنيفة أن الثمنية بالاصطلاح فتبطل لزوال الموجب فيبقى البيع بلا ثمن والعقد إنما تناول عينها بصفة الثمنية وقد انعدمت بخلاف انقطاع الرطب فإنه يعود غالباً في العام القابل بخلاف النحاس فإنه بالكساد رجع إلى أصله فكان الغالب عدم العود والكساد لغة كما في المصباح من كسد الشيء يكسد من باب فتل لم ينفق لقلة الرغبات فهو كاسد وكسد يتعدى بالهمزة فيقال أكسده الله وكسدت السوق فهي كاسد بغير هاء في الصحاح وبالهاء في التهذيب، ويقال أصل الكساد الفساد اهـ. وفقهاً أن يترك المعاملة بها في جميع البلاد وإن كانت تروج في بعض البلاد لا يبطل لكنه تعيب إذا لم ترج في بلدهم فتخير البائع إذا شاء أخذه وإن شاء أخذ قيمته. وحد الانقطاع أن يوجد في السوق وإن كان يوجد في يد الصيارفة وفي البيوت؛ هكذا في الرواية . لا وأما وفي فتح القدير : ما ذكر للكساد ذكره في العيون وقالوا: إنه على قول محمد. على قولهما فلا . وينبغي أن ينتفي البيع بالكساد في تلك البلدة التي وقع فيها البيع بناء على اختلافهم في بيع الفلس بالفلسين ؛ عندهما يجوز اعتباراً لاصطلاح بعض الناس، وعند محمد لا يجوز اعتباراً لاصطلاح الكل فالكاسد يجب أن يكون على هذا القياس أيضاً، ومثله في الانقطاع والفلوس النافقة إذا كسدت كذلك اهـ. قيد بالكساد ومثله الانقطاع لأنها لو نقصت قيمتها قبل القبض فالبيع على حاله بالإجماع ولا يتخير البائع وعكسه لو غلت قيمتها وازدادت فكذلك البيع على حاله ولا يتخير المشتري ويطالب بألف بذلك المعيار الذي كان وقت البيع ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي المصباح نفقت الدراهم نفقاً من باب تعب نقدت، ويتعدى بالهمزة فيقال أنفقتها قيدنا بكونها لم تقبض لأن البائع لو قبضها ثم كسدت فلا عن المحيط : . دلال باع متاع الغير بإذنه بدراهم معلومة واستوفاها له . وفي الخلاصة : يوجب ، يوسف لا وفي الذخيرة : الانقطاع كالكساد وحد الانقطاع أن لا يوجد في السوق وإن كان يوجد في يد الصيارفة فليس بمنقطع والأول أصح اهـ. ذكره الغزي .اهـ. قوله : (وحكم الدراهم كذلك) قال الرملي : يريد به الدراهم التي لم يغلب عليها الغش كما هو ظاهر فعلى هذا لا يختص هذا الحكم بغالب الغش ولا بالفلوس فالتنصيص عليهما دون الدراهم الجيدة لغلبة الكساد فيهما دونها تأمل . قوله: (وقالوا أنه على قول محمد الخ) قال في النهر : واعترضهم في الحواشي السعدية بأن محمداً لا يقول بأن الكساد الفساد فكيف يستقيم ذلك على قوله فليتأمل. أقول : وكذا أبو يقول به أيضاً كما قد علمت فكيف يكتفي للفساد بالكساد في تلك البلدة على قوله. ثم رأيت بعد التأمل أن مما يجب المصير إليه في الجواب أن ما في العيون مبني على ما جرى عليه في المبسوط والأسرار وشرح الطحاوي من أن الفساد بالكساد في الفلوس قول الكل وأن الخلاف الأول مقصور على الدراهم المغشوشة، وسوى القدوري بين الكل وهو الوجه إذ لا فرق يظهر، ولم أر من أفصح البحر الرائق/ ج٦ م٢٢
His statement: 'And if one buys with them or with current copper coins something and they become defunct, the sale is void.' That is, he bought with coins predominantly adulterated or with copper coins, and each was current. The sale is permissible because of the customary establishment of thamanīya, and there is no need for specification due to their being attached to price. Then if the buyer has not delivered them to the seller and they become defunct, the sale is void. Discontinuation from people's hands is like defunctness, and the ruling is similar. If he bought with silver coins and they become defunct or discontinued, the sale is void, and the buyer must return the sold item if still extant, or its like if it perished and was fungible, otherwise its value. If the sale was not yet concluded, there is no ruling for this sale at all. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The two said: The sale is not void because what is impossible is only delivery after defunctness, and that does not necessitate invalidity due to the possibility of return to currency, as if one bought something with fresh dates and they became scarce. And if it is not void and delivery is impossible, its value is obligatory; according to Abu Yusuf it is the value on the day of sale, and according to Muhammad the value on the day of defunctness, which is the last day people transact with them. In al-Dhakhira, the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement. ... And the definition of discontinuity (inqitā') is that it is found in the market; even if found in the hands of money-changers and in houses, it is not discontinued. ... The text continues with further discussion on the difference of opinion and definitions.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a sale becomes void (bāṭil) if the currency used becomes defunct (kasād) before delivery. The matn states that the sale is void. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the sale is void because the quality of being currency (thamanīya) is lost, and the contract was based on that quality. The buyer must return the sold item or its value. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the sale is not void because the impossibility is only temporary; the coins might become current again, similar to fresh dates going out of season. Instead, the buyer must pay the value of the coins. They differ on the valuation date: Abu Yusuf says the day of sale, Muhammad says the day of defunctness. The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view according to al-Dhakhira, though some later scholars favor Muhammad's view. The text also defines defunctness (kasād) as the coins being completely out of circulation in all countries, and discontinuity (inqitā') as not being found in the market even if available with money-changers. A reduction in value does not void the sale by consensus. The discussion also touches on whether the same ruling applies to good silver coins, and a nuance regarding the dispute over selling copper coins for two copper coins.

Key Terms
كساد (kasād): The state when coins are no longer accepted in all countries, i.e., completely out of circulation.
انقطاع (inqiṭāʿ): Discontinuation of coins from the market, i.e., they cannot be found in the market even if they exist with money-changers or in homes.
ثمنية (thamanīya): The quality or attribute of being currency (thaman), i.e., being accepted as a medium of exchange.
نافق (nāfiq): Coins that are currently accepted and 'passing' in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa), what is the effect on a sale if the currency becomes defunct before delivery?
A) The sale is void and the buyer returns the item
B) The sale is valid and the buyer pays the value of the coins on the day of defunctness
C) The sale is valid and the buyer pays the value of the coins on the day of sale
D) The sale is valid and the buyer pays nothing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the relied-upon view is that a sale is not voided by the defunctness of currency before delivery; instead, the buyer must pay the value of the currency on the day of sale.

Sale of Fulus upon Kasād or Inqiṭāʿ: The Hanafi Position and Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Commentary on the matn regarding sale of fulus upon disuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sale with disused fulus and explain the dispute between Muhammad and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Fulus lose their legal status as price when disused; sale becomes void unless specifically identified.

When copper coins (fulus) fall out of circulation, does the sale automatically become void? Hanafi scholars differ on this question. In this lesson, we examine the ruling in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, focusing on the distinction between circulating (nāfiqa) and disused (kāsida) fulus, and the legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Sale with fulus: valid if circulating (nāfiqa), void if disused (kāsida) according to the muftā bihi.
The ruling changes based on the status of the fulus at the time of delivery.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is based on the attribute of thumaniyyah (being a price) which is established by convention. When convention ceases, the attribute vanishes.
This is a rational proof (istihsan), not a text from Quran or Sunnah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The contract was for the coin qua price; if it loses that attribute, the price becomes unknown, invalidating the sale.
Contrast with the interruption of fresh dates which return seasonally; copper does not return to being a standard currency.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree: they hold that kasād alone does not void; it only gives an option if the coin is not current in the specific town.
Their reasoning: the convention of some people suffices to maintain thumaniyyah.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The muftā bihi (operative ruling) is Muhammad's view, as a concession to people (rafqan bi al-nās). The sale becomes fasid and the seller has an option.
Fatwa is given on Muhammad's statement despite the disagreement.

كتاب الصرف. المحيط والتتمة والحقائق : بقول محمد يفتي رفقاً بالناس ولأبي حنيفة أن الثمنية بالاصطلاح فتبطل لزوال الموجب فيبقى البيع بلا ثمن والعقد إنما تناول عينها بصفة الثمنية وقد انعدمت بخلاف انقطاع Book of Sarf. Al-Muhit, al-Tatimmah, and al-Haqa'iq: By the statement of Muhammad, a fatwa is given as a concession to people. And for Abu Hanifa, the attribute of being a price (thumaniyyah) is by convention, so it becomes void due to the removal of the cause, leaving the sale without a price. The contract only dealt with the specific coin with the quality of being a price, and that has ceased to exist, unlike the interruption of fresh dates which usually return the following year, unlike copper which returns to its original state by disuse, so the norm is non-return. Kasād linguistically, as in al-Misbah: from kasada yaksidu from the pattern of fata la, meaning it did not sell due to lack of demand, so it is kāsid. It can be transitive with the hamza: Aqsadahu Allah. The market is kāsida, without tā' in al-Sihah, with tā' in al-Tahdhib. It is said the origin of kasād is corruption. End. Juristically, it means leaving transactions with them in all lands; even if they circulate in some lands it does not void the sale, but it is a defect if they do not circulate in their land, giving the seller the option: if he wants to take it, or take its value. The definition of inqiṭāʿ is that it is not found in the market, even if it is found in the hands of money-changers and in houses; thus in the riwāyah. Not. As for. In Fath al-Qadir: What is mentioned for kasād was mentioned in al-Uyun and they said it is according to Muhammad's statement. According to his two companions, no. And it should be that the sale is voided by kasād in that town where the sale took place, based on their difference in selling one fals for two fals; according to the two it is permissible considering the convention of some people, and according to Muhammad it is not permissible considering the convention of all. So the disused (kāsid) should be analogized similarly, and likewise in interruption (inqiṭāʿ) and circulating fulus when they become disused. End. He restricted to kasād and likewise interruption, because if their value decreased before delivery, the sale remains as is by consensus and the seller has no option, and conversely if their value increased, similarly the sale remains and the buyer has no option, and he is demanded with a thousand by that standard which was at the time of sale; thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Misbah: na-qat dirhams nafaqan from the pattern of ta'iba, meaning were current, transitive with hamza: anfaqtu. We restricted to them not being taken, because if the seller took them and then they became disused, then... from al-Muhit: A broker sold someone's goods with permission for known dirhams and took them... something for him. In al-Khulasah: obligates, Yusuf does not. In al-Dhakhirah: Inqiṭāʿ is like kasād; the definition of inqiṭāʿ is that it is not found in the market, even if found in the hands of money-changers, so it is not interrupted, and the first is more correct. End, mentioned by al-Ghazi. End. His statement: (The ruling of dirhams is likewise) al-Ramli said: He means dirhams that are not predominantly adulterated, as is apparent. Thus, this ruling is not specific to predominantly adulterated dirhams nor to fulus, so specifying them rather than good dirhams is due to the prevalence of kasād in them, not others. Reflect. His statement: (And they said it is according to Muhammad's statement etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And they objected in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah that Muhammad does not say that kasād is corruption, so how can this be according to his statement? So ponder. I say: Likewise Abu Yusuf also holds it as you know, so how can he suffice with kasād in that town for corruption according to his statement? Then after reflection, I see that what is necessary to adopt in the answer is that what is in al-Uyun is based on what is established in al-Mabsut, al-Asrar, and Sharh al-Tahawi: that corruption by kasād in fulus is the statement of all, and the first difference is restricted to adulterated dirhams. Al-Quduri equated between all, which is the appropriate view since no difference is apparent, and I have not seen anyone express clearly. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol. 6 p.22. And in al-Bazzaziyyah, hire (ijarah) is like sale and debt upon this, and in marriage, the value of those dirhams is obligatory, and if some of the price was paid but not some, the remainder is void. His statement: (And sale with circulating fulus is valid even if not specified, because they are known properties and they become prices by convention, so sale with them is permissible and they are owed as a liability like the two metallic currencies, and they are not specified even if specified like currency, except if they said 'we intended to attach the ruling to the specific ones', then the contract is attached to the specific ones, unlike the case of selling one fals for two specific fals where specification occurs without explicit statement because if not specified the sale would be void. This is according to the two; as for according to Muhammad, they are not specified even if they explicitly state, and the origin is that the general public's convention is not invalidated by their convention to the contrary according to him, while according to the two it is invalidated in their regard as we have presented. His statement: (And with disused (kāsida) it is not valid until they specify them) because they are commodities, so specification is necessary.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the rulings of sale with fulus (copper coins) when they become kāsida (disused) or inqata'a (unavailable). The passage type is an expansion probe, as the commentator expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the legal reasoning and intra-Hanafi disputes. The core issue is whether kasād (lack of circulation) or inqiṭāʿ (unavailability in the market) before delivery voids the sale. The commentator cites multiple works: al-Muhit, al-Tatimmah, al-Haqa'iq, Fath al-Qadir, al-Uyun, etc. He first clarifies the linguistic and technical definition of kasād. Linguistically, it means lack of demand; technically, it means that the coin is no longer used as a medium of exchange in all countries, even if it circulates in some. If it does not circulate in the specific town of the transaction, the seller has an option to either take back the coin or demand its value. The definition of inqiṭāʿ is that the coin is not found in the market, even if it exists in the hands of money-changers or in houses. The commentator notes a dispute: according to Muhammad, kasād voids the sale because the attribute of thumaniyyah (being a price) has ceased, leaving the contract without a price—like the example of copper returning to its original state. According to Abu Hanifa as stated in some works, the same conclusion is reached through istihsan, though there is also a view that it is based on the dispute over selling one fals for two fals: the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) allow it considering the convention of some people, while Muhammad considers the convention of all, so the kāsid is analogized accordingly. The commentator also distinguishes between kasād and mere fluctuation in value: if the value drops or rises before delivery, the sale remains valid and neither party has an option—only actual kasād or inqiṭāʿ affects the contract. He then discusses the ruling for dirhams that are predominantly adulterated, noting that some restrict the kasād ruling to them, but al-Ramli extends it to all fulus. Finally, the passage covers the validity of sale with circulating fulus (nafiqa): they are valid as consideration and are like dirhams and dinars in that they are owed as a debt and need not be specified unless the parties explicitly intend to deal with specific coins. The exception is selling one fals for two specific fals, where specification is required to avoid usury (ribā), and there is a difference between the two companions and Muhammad on whether specification can occur through explicit intent. For disused fulus (kāsida), however, specification is mandatory because they are merely commodities.

Key Terms
الفلوس (al-fulūs): Copper coins used as small currency; they are treated as prices (athmān) by convention but not legal tender by silver/gold standard.
الكساد (al-kasād): The state of a coin no longer being accepted as money in all countries; it becomes unsaleable as currency.
الانقطاع (al-inqiṭāʿ): The unavailability of the coin in the market, even if it exists in private hands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muftā bihi position in this passage, what is the consequence for a sale of fulus if they become disused (kasād) before delivery?
A) Sale becomes void without compensation for seller.
B) Seller has option to either void the sale and take back the goods or demand the value.
C) Sale remains valid and seller must accept the disused fulus.
D) Only the buyer can void the sale.

Key Takeaway
The status of fulus as prices depends on general convention; when convention ceases, the sale becomes fasid unless the coins are specifically identified.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
The Loan of Fulus upon Kasād: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Repayment
Commentary on a matn ruling about fulus loans upon kasād

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on repaying a loan of fulus when the coins become disused, explain the qiyas used by each companion, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
When fulus become disused, a loan of fulus must be repaid in value, not in the same number, according to the mufta bihi.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical scenario in the Hanafi school: a loan of copper coins (fulus) that lose their status as currency (kasād). Should the borrower return the same number of coins, even if worthless? Or should they pay the value, and if so, valued at what date? The three major Hanafi imams – Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani – each gave a different answer, and the later fatwa adopted Muhammad's view as a concession to people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State Abu Hanifa's view (return the same number), Abu Yusuf's (value at receipt), Muhammad's (value at kasād).
The core dispute is whether mithl (like) means number or value.
2
Understand the underlying principle
Abu Hanifa views a loan as a borrowing (i'ārah) of the use, so returning the same number fulfills the obligation. The companions view the loan as an exchange of value, so similarity is lost when the coins cease to be currency.
This principle difference leads to different qiyas analogies.
3
Trace the qiyas analogy
The companions analogize to ghasb (usurpation) of a mithli commodity that becomes unavailable. All agree value is due, but the valuation date differs: Abu Hanifa uses lawsuit day, Abu Yusuf uses usurpation (here receipt), Muhammad uses interruption (here kasād).
The parallel is explicitly drawn in the text.
4
Analyze the tarjih
The fatwa is on Muhammad's view as a concession to people (rafqan bi al-nās), because the value at kasād is usually lower, benefiting the borrower. Also, practical difficulty: value at kasād is harder to determine, but community welfare is prioritized.
Al-Khaniyyah, al-Fatawa al-Sughra, and al-Bazzaziyyah all state this tarjih.
5
Apply to a hypothetical
Given a loan of fulus that become disused, determine the mufta bihi obligation: pay the value as of the date the coins became disused.
Other applications include ijārah and debt, but marriage has a different rule per the passage.

ثمن والمردود لا ففاتت المماثلة فصار كما لو استقرض مثلياً فانقطع لكن عند أبي يوسف عليه القيمة يوم القبض، وعند محمد الكساد، وقول محمد أنظر في حق المستقرض لأن قيمته يوم الانقطاع أقل. وكذا في حق يوم المقرض بالنظر إلى قول أبي حنيفة، وقول أبي يوسف أيسر لأن قيمته يوم القبض معلومة ويوم الكساد لا تعرف إلا بحرج ولأبي حنيفة أن القرض إعارة وموجبها رد العين معنى وذلك يتحقق برد مثله والثمنية زيادة فيه في المنقطع المساوي حكمه للكساد؛ كذا نبه عليه شيخنا أبو السعود قوله : ( وفي فتح القدير وأما إذا استقرض دراهم غالبة
Specify them, and if the fulus of a loan become disused (kasāda), the like (mithl) of them must be returned. And if someone bought something for half a dirham worth of fulus, valid, then they became disused before he paid them to the owner of the goods, the sale is not void because the right of receipt is for him. End. And in al-Bazzaziyyah, attributed to al-Muntaqa: If fulus of a loan increased or decreased in value, according to the first Imam and the second initially, he is not liable for anything other than them. And the second later said: He must pay their value in dirhams on the day of sale and receipt, and the fatwa is upon that. Likewise in al-Dhakhirah, al-Khulasah by al-Izz, and attributed to al-Muntaqa, and in Fatawa Qadikhan: He must return the like (mithl). Likewise al-Asbijabi mentioned, said: He does not look to value, but he gave the example of selling one hundred fulus for one dirham. And their statement from al-Muntaqa, 'He must pay their value in dirhams on the day of sale and receipt' is perhaps distributive: his saying 'day of sale' refers to the sale, and his saying 'day of receipt' refers to the loan. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: hire (ijarah) is like sale and debt upon this, and in marriage, the value of those dirhams is obligatory, and if some of the price was paid but not some, the remainder is void. His statement: (And sale with circulating fulus is valid even if not specified, because they are known properties and they become prices by convention, so sale with them is permissible and they are owed as a liability like the two metallic currencies, and they are not specified even if specified like currency, except if they said 'we intended to attach the ruling to the specific ones', then the contract is attached to the specific ones, unlike the case of selling one fals for two specific fals where specification occurs without explicit statement because if not specified the sale would be void. This is according to the two; as for according to Muhammad, they are not specified even if they explicitly state, and the origin is that the general public's convention is not invalidated by their convention to the contrary according to him, while according to the two it is invalidated in their regard as we have presented. His statement: (And with disused (kāsida) it is not valid until they specify them) because they are commodities, so specification is necessary. His statement: (And if the fulus of a loan become disused, the like must be returned) i.e., the number according to Abu Hanifa. And the two said: He must return their value because it is difficult to return them as received, because what was taken is a price and what is returned is not, so similarity is lost, so it becomes like a loan of a mithli (fungible) that becomes unavailable—but according to Abu Yusuf, he pays the value on the day of receipt, and according to Muhammad, on the day of kasād. Muhammad's statement is more considerate towards the borrower because the value on the day of interruption is less. Likewise for the lender's right, regarding Abu Hanifa's statement, Abu Yusuf's statement is easier because the value on the day of receipt is known, while on the day of kasād it is not known except with difficulty. For Abu Hanifa, the loan is a borrowing (i'ārah) and its requirement is to return the entity in meaning, and that is realized by returning its like, and being a price (thumaniyyah) is an extra attribute. The difference is based on the difference regarding one who usurps a mithli like fresh dates and then they become unavailable from people: its value is obligatory by consensus, but for Abu Hanifa it is the value on the day of lawsuit, for Abu Yusuf the day of usurpation, for Muhammad the day of interruption. In al-Khaniyyah, al-Fatawa al-Sughra, and al-Bazzaziyyah: The fatwa is on Muhammad's statement as a concession to people. In al-Misbah: al-fals (the coin) is what is used in transactions; its plural in small quantity is aflus, in large quantity fulūs. In Fath al-Qadir: And if one borrows dirhams predominantly adulterated, Abu Yusuf said: analogically to Abu Hanifa's statement, he must return the like of them, and I do not narrate that from him, but according to his narration that he said this about... and Allah is the giver of success. His statement: (And in al-Bazzaziyyah: hire is like sale and debt upon this) suggests connections of inflation and deflation, but it is not so because al-Bazzazi only mentioned that in the context of interrupted (munqati') which is equal in ruling to kasād; thus our teacher Abu al-Su'ud pointed it out. His statement: (And in Fath al-Qadir: And if one borrows predominantly adulterated dirhams...)
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the rulings for fulus, now focusing on loans (qard). The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute, as it presents the differing positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the repayment of a loan of fulus when the coin becomes kāsida (disused) or inqata'a (unavailable). The matn statement 'ولو كسدت أفلس القرض يجب رد مثلها' (If the fulus of a loan become disused, the like must be returned) is interpreted by Abu Hanifa as meaning the same number (mithl in quantity). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue for value (qīmah) because the receipt was of coins that were considered prices, and upon kasād, the coins lose their price attribute, so returning the same number does not achieve similarity (mumāthala) — rather, the borrower should pay the value as of a specific date. Abu Yusuf says value on the day of receipt (qabd), Muhammad says value on the day of kasād. The commentary explains that this dispute is based on a parallel dispute in the case of ghaṣb (usurpation) of a mithli (e.g., fresh dates) that becomes unavailable: all agree that value is due, but they differ on the date — Abu Hanifa uses the day of lawsuit, Abu Yusuf the day of usurpation, Muhammad the day of interruption. The passage also explains the reasoning: Abu Hanifa views a loan as a kind of i'ārah (borrowing for use), where the borrower must return the entity in a meaningful sense, which is fulfilled by returning its like (number); the attribute of being a price is an extra, not essential. The two companions view the transaction as a purchase of value. The muftā bihi (fatwa position) is Muhammad's view, as a concession to people (rafqan bi al-nās) because the value on the day of kasād is often lower, benefiting the borrower. The passage also cites related works: al-Bazzaziyyah, al-Muntaqa, Fatawa Qadikhan, and others, noting that in some contexts (like ijārah and sale debts), the ruling follows the same pattern, but for marriage (nikāh) the value of those dirhams is obligatory. The final part clarifies that the discussion on inflation/deflation in the Bazzaziyyah is specifically about munqati' (interrupted) coins, not about mere price fluctuation.

Key Terms
القرض (al-qarḍ): A contract loaning fungible (mithli) property to be repaid with its like (mithl) in quantity and quality.
المثل (al-mithl): The like in kind, quantity, and quality; for fulus, the number (count) is considered mithl because they are fungible (mithliyyāt).
الكساد (al-kasād): The state of fulus no longer being accepted as currency; they become unsaleable in the market.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muftā bihi (fatwa) position mentioned in the passage, if a person borrowed fulus and then the fulus become kāsida before repayment, what is the borrower obligated to pay?
A) The same number (mithl) of fulus, even if disused.
B) The value of the fulus at the time of receipt (qabd).
C) The value of the fulus at the time of kasād (disuse).
D) The value of the fulus at the time of the court lawsuit.

Key Takeaway
In loans of fulus, if the coins become disused, the borrower must pay the value at the date of disuse (kasād) according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale with Current Fulus: Validity and Specification
Ruling on sale with current fulus and specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sale with current fulus without specification and explain when specification is required or overridden by public convention.

Core Idea
Current fulus are like gold and silver: valid without specification; only explicit intent to attach to specific coins binds.

Fulus (copper coins) have been used as currency by custom. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on selling with current fulus (al-fulus al-nafiqah). The key issue is whether the coins must be specified in the contract. We'll see that generally they are not specified, but there are exceptions and an important intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn on the role of public convention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
State that sale with current fulus is valid even without specifying the individual coins. The basis is that fulus have become de facto currency by convention ('urf).
2
Understand the basis
Current fulus are known properties and are treated like gold and silver dirhams; they are owed in the liability (dhimmah) and not attached to specific coins unless intended.
3
Note the exception
If the parties explicitly state that they intend to tie the contract to specific coins, then those coins become specified and the contract attaches to them.
This is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn).
4
Distinguish a special case
If a person sells a specific fils for two specific fils, the coins are automatically specified even without explicit mention, because otherwise the sale would be invalid (due to ambiguity in the exchange rate).
This is an exception to the general rule of non-specification.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad holds that even if parties explicitly specify, it does not take effect because public convention ('urf 'amm) cannot be overridden by private agreement. The Sahibayn hold that it can be overridden.
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn: specification can be effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح البيع بالفلوس النافقة وإن لم تتعين لأنها أموال معلومة وصارت أثماناً بالاصطلاح فجاز بها البيع ووجبت في الذمة كالنقدين ولا تتعين وإن عينها كالنقد إلا إذا قالا أردنا تعليق الحكم بعينها فحينئذ يتعلق العقد بعينها بخلاف ما إذا باع فلساً بفلسين بأعيانهما حيث يتعين من غير تصريح لأنه لو لم يتعين لفسد البيع. وهذا على قولهما، وأما على قول محمد لا يتعين وإن صرحا وأصله أن اصطلاح العامة لا يبطل باصطلاحهما على خلافه عنده، وعندهما يبطل في حقهما كما قدمناه) قوله: (وبالكاسدة لا حتى يعينها) لأنها سلع فلا بد من تعينها) قوله: (ولو اشترى شيئاً بنصف درهم فلوس صح وعليه فلوس تباع بنصف درهم وعلى هذا لو قال بثلث درهم أو بربعه أو بدائق فلوس أو بقيراط فلوس لأن التبايع بهذا الطريق متعارف في القليل معلوم بين الناس لا تفاوت فيه فلا يؤدي إلى النزاع. قيد بما دون الدرهم لأنه لو اشترى بدرهم فلوس لا يجوز عند محمد، أو بدرهمين فلوس لا يجوز عند محمد لعدم العرف، وجوزه أبو يوسف في الكل للعرف وهو الأصح؛ كذا في الكافي والمجتبى والدائق سدس درهم والقيراط نصف السدس) قوله: (ومن أعطى صيرفيا درهماً فقال أعطني به نصف درهم فلوس ونصفاً إلا حبة صح لأن قابل الدرهم بنصف درهم فلوس وبنصف درهم إلا حبة من الفضة فيكون نصف درهم إلا حبة بمقابلة الفضة ونصف درهم وحبة بمقابلة الفلوس. قيد بقوله «به لأنه لو قال أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وبنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة بطل في الكل على قياس قوله وعندهما صح في الفلوس وبطل فيما قابل الفضة لأن الفساد هما عند التفصيل يتقدر بقدر المفسد، وعنده يتعدى وأصله أن العقد يتكرر عنده بتكرار اللفظ، وعندهما بتفصيل الثمن حتى لو قال أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وأعطني بنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة جاز في الفلوس وبطل في الفضة بالإجماع فهنا صور: الأولى مسألة الكتاب أعطني نصف درهم فلوس ونصفاً إلا حبة صح اتفاقاً. الثانية أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وبنصفه نصفاً) به الغش الخ) اعلم أن تقييد الاختلاف في رد المثل أو القيمة بالكساد يشير إلى أنها إذا غلت أو رخصت وجب رد المثل بالاتفاق وقد مر نظيره فيما إذا اشترى بغالب الغش أو بفلوس نافقة. واعلم أنه استفيد من كلامه أن تقييد الكاسد بأفلس القرض ليس احترازياً بدليل أنه حكى الخلاف في رد المثل أو القيمة فيما إذا كان القرض الذي كسد مما غلب غشه.
He said: (And the sale is valid with current fulus even if not specified, because they are known properties and became prices by convention, so sale is permissible with them and they are owed in the liability like the two metals (gold and silver), and they are not specified even if specified like the two metals, except if they said: 'We intended to attach the ruling to their specific identity'; then the contract is attached to their identity, contrary to if he sold a fils for two fils with their specific identities, where they become specified without explicit mention because if not specified, the sale would be invalid. This is according to them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for the opinion of Muhammad, they are not specified even if they explicitly state, and the basis is that the convention of the common people is not invalidated by their convention to the contrary according to him, while according to them both it is invalidated in their case, as we have presented.) He said: (And with obsolete (fulus), no, until he specifies them) because they are commodities, so specification is necessary. He said: (And if he bought something with half a dirham of fulus, it is valid, and he must pay fulus that can be sold for half a dirham. Similarly, if he said 'with a third of a dirham' or 'a quarter' or 'sixths of fulus' or 'a qirat of fulus', because trading in this way is customary in small amounts, known among people, with no variation, so it does not lead to dispute. It is restricted to less than a dirham, because if he bought with a dirham of fulus, it is not permissible according to Muhammad, or with two dirhams of fulus, it is not permissible according to Muhammad due to lack of custom, while Abu Yusuf permitted all due to custom, and that is the most correct. Thus in al-Kafi and al-Mujtaba. The da'iq is a sixth of a dirham, and the qirat is half of the sixth.) He said: (And if someone gave a money-changer a dirham and said: 'Give me for it half a dirham of fulus and half except a habbah,' it is valid, because he exchanged the dirham for half a dirham of fulus and for half a dirham except a habbah of silver. So half a dirham except a habbah is in exchange for the silver, and half a dirham and a habbah is in exchange for the fulus. It is restricted by his saying 'for it', because if he said: 'Give me for half of it fulus and for half of it half except a habbah,' it would be invalid entirely in qiyas to his statement, while according to them both it is valid for the fulus and invalid for the part corresponding to silver, because corruption, according to them in detail, is restricted to the extent of the corrupting factor, while according to him it extends. The basis is that the contract is repeated according to him by repetition of the wording, and according to them by detailing the price. So if he said: 'Give me for half of it fulus and give me for half of it half except a habbah,' it is valid for the fulus and invalid for the silver by consensus. There are three forms: the first is the matter of the text: 'Give me half a dirham of fulus and half except a habbah,' valid by agreement. The second: 'Give me for half of it fulus and for half of it half...' with the excess silver...) Know that restricting the dispute about repayment of the like or value to kasād indicates that if they rise or fall in price, repayment of the like is obligatory by agreement, and its analogy has passed in the case of purchasing with predominantly adulterated coins or with current fulus. And know that it is understood from his speech that restricting 'kasād' to the loan of fulus is not exclusive, as evidence that he narrated the dispute in repaying the like or value in the case of a loan that became obsolete while being predominantly adulterated.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on sale with fulus (copper coins) in the Hanafi school. It begins with the validity of sale using current fulus (al-fulus al-nafiqah) even without specifying the specific coins, because they are considered known properties and have become de facto currency (athman) by convention (istilah). Like gold and silver dirhams, they are generally not specified in the contract unless the parties explicitly state their intent to tie the contract to specific coins. A distinction is made: if one sells a specific fils for two specific fils, the coins are automatically specified due to the nature of the transaction (otherwise invalidity follows). This ruling is according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn). However, according to Muhammad alone, specification does not occur even if explicitly stated, because the general public's convention (that fulus are not specified) cannot be overridden by the parties' private agreement. Next, the passage addresses obsolete fulus (al-fulus al-kasidah). Since they are merely commodities (sila') and not currency, they must be specified in the contract. The third section covers fractional purchases: buying something for half a dirham worth of fulus, or a third, a quarter, a sixth (da'iq), or a qirat (half a sixth) of fulus. Such transactions are valid because they are customary for small amounts and clear to people. However, if the amount is a full dirham or two dirhams of fulus, Muhammad prohibits it due to lack of custom, while Abu Yusuf permits all such transactions due to custom, and this is the relied-upon position (asahh). Finally, the passage discusses a money-changer scenario: someone gives a dirham and asks for half a dirham of fulus and half a dirham minus one habbah (grain of silver). This is valid because the transaction effectively exchanges the dirham for the specified amounts of fulus and silver. The restriction lies in the wording: if the request is phrased as 'give me for half of it fulus and for half of it half except a habbah,' the ruling differs. According to Abu Hanifa, the entire contract is invalid because invalidity spreads; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it is valid for the fulus part and invalid only for the silver part, as corruption is limited to the corrupt element. This hinges on whether the contract is repeated by repetition of the wording or by detailing the price. The marginal note at the end clarifies that the dispute about repaying the like or value only applies in cases of kasād (obsoletion), not price fluctuation (ghala/rukhs), where repayment of the like is unanimous. It also notes that the ruling applies similarly to loans with predominantly adulterated silver coins.

Key Terms
الفلوس النافقة (al-fulus al-nafiqah): Current copper coins that are actively used as currency by public convention.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specification of the exact coins in a contract; under default, fulus are not specified unless explicitly intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a buyer uses current fulus to purchase an item without specifying the coins, the sale is:
A) Invalid because the fulus are not specified.
B) Valid and the fulus are owed in the liability.
C) Valid only if the fulus are weighed.
D) Invalid unless the parties explicitly agree to generic coins.

Key Takeaway
Current fulus are valid without specification; explicit specification only binds if intended, and according to Sahibayn it is effective over public convention.

Sale with Obsolete Fulus: Commodity Status Requires Specification
Ruling on sale with obsolete fulus requiring specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ruling on sale with obsolete fulus and explain why specification is required.

Core Idea
Obsolete fulus are mere commodities; the sale is invalid unless the exact coins are specified.

When fulus become obsolete (al-fulus al-kasidah) – no longer accepted as currency – they lose their status as de facto money and revert to being ordinary commodities. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on using such obsolete coins in a sale. The key point is that they must be specified in the contract, otherwise the sale is invalid.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Obsolete fulus (kasidah) cannot be used in a sale unless they are specified.
The text says: 'وبالكاسدة لا حتى يعينها' – and with obsolete, no, until he specifies them.
2
Understand the reason
Obsolete fulus are no longer considered currency (athman); they are mere commodities (sila') which require specification to avoid ambiguity (gharar).
3
Contrast with current fulus
Current fulus are valid without specification; obsolete fulus require specification. This distinction hinges on whether the coins function as currency or as goods.
4
Apply the ruling
If a person tries to buy an item with obsolete fulus without specifying which coins, the contract is invalid (batil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
