A distinction is made: if one sells a specific fils for two specific fils, the coins are automatically specified due to the nature of the transaction (otherwise invalidity follows). This ruling is according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn). However, according to Muhammad alone, specification does not occur even if explicitly stated, because the general public's convention (that fulus are not specified) cannot be overridden by the parties' private agreement. Next, the passage addresses obsolete fulus (al-fulus al-kasidah). Since they are merely commodities (sila') and not currency, they must be specified in the contract. The third section covers fractional purchases: buying something for half a dirham worth of fulus, or a third, a quarter, a sixth (da'iq), or a qirat (half a sixth) of fulus. Such transactions are valid because they are customary for small amounts and clear to people. However, if the amount is a full dirham or two dirhams of fulus, Muhammad prohibits it due to lack of custom, while Abu Yusuf permits all such transactions due to custom, and this is the relied-upon position (asahh). Finally, the passage discusses a money-changer scenario: someone gives a dirham and asks for half a dirham of fulus and half a dirham minus one habbah (grain of silver). This is valid because the transaction effectively exchanges the dirham for the specified amounts of fulus and silver. The restriction lies in the wording: if the request is phrased as 'give me for half of it fulus and for half of it half except a habbah,' the ruling differs. According to Abu Hanifa, the entire contract is invalid because invalidity spreads; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it is valid for the fulus part and invalid only for the silver part, as corruption is limited to the corrupt element. This hinges on whether the contract is repeated by repetition of the wording or by detailing the price. The marginal note at the end clarifies that the dispute about repaying the like or value only applies in cases of kasād (obsoletion), not price fluctuation (ghala/rukhs), where repayment of the like is unanimous. It also notes that the ruling applies similarly to loans with predominantly adulterated silver coins.

Key Terms
الفلوس الكاسدة (al-fulus al-kasidah): Obsolete copper coins no longer accepted as currency, treated as mere commodities (sila').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on selling a commodity for obsolete fulus without specifying the coins?
A) The sale is valid without specification.
B) The sale is invalid unless the parties agree to treat them as currency.
C) The sale is invalid unless the coins are specified.
D) The sale is valid if the amount is less than a dirham.

Key Takeaway
Obsolete fulus are commodities; they must be specified in the sale contract or the sale is invalid.

Fractional Fulus Transactions: Custom and the Dispute on Full Dirhams
Rulings on fractional fulus transactions and the dispute on amounts of a dirham or more.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the validity of fractional fulus transactions (less than a dirham) and explain the dispute regarding transactions of one dirham or more.

Core Idea
Small fractional fulus amounts (half, third, quarter, sixth, qirat) are valid by custom; full dirhams or more are disputed between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

This lesson covers the permissibility of purchasing items with fractional amounts of fulus, such as half a dirham, a third, a quarter, or smaller fractions like da'iq (1/6 dirham) and qirat (1/12 dirham). The Hanafi school generally validates these because they are customary and well-known, but when the amount reaches a full dirham or more, a dispute arises between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
List the permissible fractional amounts
The text mentions half a dirham, third, quarter, da'iq (1/6), and qirat (1/12) of dirham worth of fulus.
2
Understand the basis of validity
These transactions are valid because they are customary (mutta'arif) in small amounts, well-known to people, and do not lead to dispute (niza').
3
Note the restriction to less than a dirham
The text says it is restricted (qayyada) to less than a dirham. For a full dirham or two dirhams, there is a dispute.
4
Identify the dispute
Imam Muhammad does not permit a full dirham or two dirhams of fulus due to lack of custom. Imam Abu Yusuf permits all such transactions due to custom, and this is the relied-upon position (asahh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح البيع بالفلوس النافقة وإن لم تتعين لأنها أموال معلومة وصارت أثماناً بالاصطلاح فجاز بها البيع ووجبت في الذمة كالنقدين ولا تتعين وإن عينها كالنقد إلا إذا قالا أردنا تعليق الحكم بعينها فحينئذ يتعلق العقد بعينها بخلاف ما إذا باع فلساً بفلسين بأعيانهما حيث يتعين من غير تصريح لأنه لو لم يتعين لفسد البيع. وهذا على قولهما، وأما على قول محمد لا يتعين وإن صرحا وأصله أن اصطلاح العامة لا يبطل باصطلاحهما على خلافه عنده، وعندهما يبطل في حقهما كما قدمناه) قوله: (وبالكاسدة لا حتى يعينها) لأنها سلع فلا بد من تعينها) قوله: (ولو اشترى شيئاً بنصف درهم فلوس صح وعليه فلوس تباع بنصف درهم وعلى هذا لو قال بثلث درهم أو بربعه أو بدائق فلوس أو بقيراط فلوس لأن التبايع بهذا الطريق متعارف في القليل معلوم بين الناس لا تفاوت فيه فلا يؤدي إلى النزاع. قيد بما دون الدرهم لأنه لو اشترى بدرهم فلوس لا يجوز عند محمد، أو بدرهمين فلوس لا يجوز عند محمد لعدم العرف، وجوزه أبو يوسف في الكل للعرف وهو الأصح؛ كذا في الكافي والمجتبى والدائق سدس درهم والقيراط نصف السدس) قوله: (ومن أعطى صيرفيا درهماً فقال أعطني به نصف درهم فلوس ونصفاً إلا حبة صح لأن قابل الدرهم بنصف درهم فلوس وبنصف درهم إلا حبة من الفضة فيكون نصف درهم إلا حبة بمقابلة الفضة ونصف درهم وحبة بمقابلة الفلوس. قيد بقوله «به لأنه لو قال أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وبنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة بطل في الكل على قياس قوله وعندهما صح في الفلوس وبطل فيما قابل الفضة لأن الفساد هما عند التفصيل يتقدر بقدر المفسد، وعنده يتعدى وأصله أن العقد يتكرر عنده بتكرار اللفظ، وعندهما بتفصيل الثمن حتى لو قال أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وأعطني بنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة جاز في الفلوس وبطل في الفضة بالإجماع فهنا صور: الأولى مسألة الكتاب أعطني نصف درهم فلوس ونصفاً إلا حبة صح اتفاقاً. الثانية أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وبنصفه نصفاً) به الغش الخ) اعلم أن تقييد الاختلاف في رد المثل أو القيمة بالكساد يشير إلى أنها إذا غلت أو رخصت وجب رد المثل بالاتفاق وقد مر نظيره فيما إذا اشترى بغالب الغش أو بفلوس نافقة. واعلم أنه استفيد من كلامه أن تقييد الكاسد بأفلس القرض ليس احترازياً بدليل أنه حكى الخلاف في رد المثل أو القيمة فيما إذا كان القرض الذي كسد مما غلب غشه.
He said: (And the sale is valid with current fulus even if not specified, because they are known properties and became prices by convention, so sale is permissible with them and they are owed in the liability like the two metals (gold and silver), and they are not specified even if specified like the two metals, except if they said: 'We intended to attach the ruling to their specific identity'; then the contract is attached to their identity, contrary to if he sold a fils for two fils with their specific identities, where they become specified without explicit mention because if not specified, the sale would be invalid. This is according to them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for the opinion of Muhammad, they are not specified even if they explicitly state, and the basis is that the convention of the common people is not invalidated by their convention to the contrary according to him, while according to them both it is invalidated in their case, as we have presented.) He said: (And with obsolete (fulus), no, until he specifies them) because they are commodities, so specification is necessary. He said: (And if he bought something with half a dirham of fulus, it is valid, and he must pay fulus that can be sold for half a dirham. Similarly, if he said 'with a third of a dirham' or 'a quarter' or 'sixths of fulus' or 'a qirat of fulus', because trading in this way is customary in small amounts, known among people, with no variation, so it does not lead to dispute. It is restricted to less than a dirham, because if he bought with a dirham of fulus, it is not permissible according to Muhammad, or with two dirhams of fulus, it is not permissible according to Muhammad due to lack of custom, while Abu Yusuf permitted all due to custom, and that is the most correct. Thus in al-Kafi and al-Mujtaba. The da'iq is a sixth of a dirham, and the qirat is half of the sixth.) He said: (And if someone gave a money-changer a dirham and said: 'Give me for it half a dirham of fulus and half except a habbah,' it is valid, because he exchanged the dirham for half a dirham of fulus and for half a dirham except a habbah of silver. So half a dirham except a habbah is in exchange for the silver, and half a dirham and a habbah is in exchange for the fulus. It is restricted by his saying 'for it', because if he said: 'Give me for half of it fulus and for half of it half except a habbah,' it would be invalid entirely in qiyas to his statement, while according to them both it is valid for the fulus and invalid for the part corresponding to silver, because corruption, according to them in detail, is restricted to the extent of the corrupting factor, while according to him it extends. The basis is that the contract is repeated according to him by repetition of the wording, and according to them by detailing the price. So if he said: 'Give me for half of it fulus and give me for half of it half except a habbah,' it is valid for the fulus and invalid for the silver by consensus. There are three forms: the first is the matter of the text: 'Give me half a dirham of fulus and half except a habbah,' valid by agreement. The second: 'Give me for half of it fulus and for half of it half...' with the excess silver...) Know that restricting the dispute about repayment of the like or value to kasād indicates that if they rise or fall in price, repayment of the like is obligatory by agreement, and its analogy has passed in the case of purchasing with predominantly adulterated coins or with current fulus. And know that it is understood from his speech that restricting 'kasād' to the loan of fulus is not exclusive, as evidence that he narrated the dispute in repaying the like or value in the case of a loan that became obsolete while being predominantly adulterated.

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on sale with fulus (copper coins) in the Hanafi school. It begins with the validity of sale using current fulus (al-fulus al-nafiqah) even without specifying the specific coins, because they are considered known properties and have become de facto currency (athman) by convention (istilah). The third section covers fractional purchases: buying something for half a dirham worth of fulus, or a third, a quarter, a sixth (da'iq), or a qirat (half a sixth) of fulus. However, if the amount is a full dirham or two dirhams of fulus, Muhammad prohibits it due to lack of custom, while Abu Yusuf permits all such transactions due to custom, and this is the relied-upon position (asahh). Finally, the passage discusses a money-changer scenario: someone gives a dirham and asks for half a dirham of fulus and half a dirham minus one habbah (grain of silver). It also notes that the ruling applies similarly to loans with predominantly adulterated silver coins.

Key Terms
الدائق (al-da'iq): A unit of currency equal to one-sixth of a dirham.
القيراط (al-qirat): A unit equal to half of a da'iq, i.e., one-twelfth of a dirham.

A) No, because the text restricts to less than a dirham.
B) Yes, because Abu Yusuf allowed it and it is the most correct view.
C) No, because only fractions are valid.
D) Yes, but only if the fulus are specified.

Key Takeaway
Fractional fulus transactions are valid by custom; for full dirhams or more, the relied-upon position is permissibility, following Abu Yusuf.

Money-Changer Transaction: Complex Wording and Contract Validity
Ruling on a complex money-changer transaction with different wordings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on contract specification and repetition to a money-changer transaction involving fulus and silver fractions.

Core Idea
In a money-changer transaction, the phrasing determines whether the contract is partially or totally invalid—the key is whether the wording repeats the contract or details the price.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a person gives a dirham to a money-changer and requests half a dirham worth of fulus and half a dirham minus a habbah (grain) of silver. The ruling depends on how the request is worded. The underlying principle involves whether the contract is considered repeated or divided, and how invalidity spreads. This case illustrates the Hanafi approach to complex transactional language and the importance of precision in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic scenario
The person gives a dirham and says: 'Give me for it half a dirham of fulus and half except a habbah.' This is valid by agreement.
2
Identify the valid phrasing
The valid wording uses 'for it' (bi) preceding the entire request. This makes the transaction an exchange of the dirham for two defined parts: fulus and silver.
The exchange is: dirham = (1/2 dirham fulus) + (1/2 dirham - 1 habbah silver).
3
Contrast with alternative phrasing
If the request is: 'Give me for half of it fulus and for half of it half except a habbah,' the ruling differs. According to Abu Hanifa, the whole contract is invalid; according to Sahibayn, it is valid for the fulus part and invalid for the silver part.
4
Analyze the underlying principles
Abu Hanifa considers that the contract is repeated by repetition of the wording, so corruption spreads to the entire contract. The Sahibayn consider that the contract is divided by the detailing of the price, so corruption is limited to the corrupt part.
5
Apply to the three forms
The text lists three forms: (1) the valid form (by agreement), (2) the alternative phrasing leading to differing rulings, and (3) a consensus case where the request is split into two separate sentences, making the fulus part valid and the silver part invalid by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specification of the exact coins in a contract; under default, fulus are not specified unless explicitly intended.

Checkpoint
In the money-changer scenario, what is the relied-upon ruling when the client says 'give me for half of it fulus and for half of it half except a habbah'?
A) The entire transaction is invalid.
B) The fulus part is valid and the silver part is invalid.
C) The entire transaction is valid.
D) The fulus part is invalid and the silver part is valid.

Key Takeaway
Precise wording in contracts is critical; the relied-upon Hanafi position confines invalidity to the corrupt part when the contract is divided by price detailing.

Repayment of Obsolete Fulus: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Rulings on repaying fulus when they become obsolete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repaying obsolete fulus and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether and when to repay value.

Core Idea
When fulus become obsolete, the borrower must repay value on the day of obsolescence — that is the mufta bihi.

What happens when a currency ceases to be legal tender after a loan? The Hanafi school has a nuanced answer, with three different positions from the founding imams. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the dispute over whether the borrower must return the exact number of coins (like) or their value, and if value, which date to use. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply analogous reasoning (qiyas) and to identify the operative ruling (mufta bihi) when circumstances change.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: what must the borrower repay when fulus become obsolete? Begin with the position of Abu Hanifa (the like) and the Sahibayn (value).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree on value but differ on the valuation date. Abu Yusuf uses the day of receipt (qabd), Muhammad uses the day of obsolescence (kasād).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the analogy (qiyas) to the case of usurping a fungible (e.g., fresh dates) that later becomes unavailable. Consensus requires value; the Sahibayn extend this to the fulus loan.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning: a loan is like a borrowing for use ('ariyah) – the substance is returned by number, and the price characteristic is incidental. The distinction between kasād and price fluctuation is critical.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: the borrower pays less under Muhammad's view (value at obsolescence, which is usually lower) than under Abu Yusuf's (value at receipt). Show how the tarjih (Muhammad's view) is chosen for kindness to people.

And if he bought something with half a dirham of fulus, it is valid; then if they became obsolete before he paid them to the owner of the goods, the sale is not invalidated because the right to take possession is his. End. In al-Bazzaziyya, referring to al-Muntaqa: The fulus appreciated or depreciated in the loan; according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) and the first statement of the Second (Abu Yusuf first opinion), he is not liable for anything else. The Second's second opinion: he must pay their value in dirhams on the day of sale and on the day of taking possession, and this is the fatwa. Similarly in al-Dhakhira and al-Khulasa. And in Fatawa Qadikhan, he must pay the like. Similarly, al-Asbijabi mentioned: he does not consider value, but he illustrated with the case of selling one hundred fulus for a dirham. Their statement from al-Muntaqa: he must pay their value in dirhams on the day of sale and on the day of taking possession, perhaps by distribution; his statement 'day of sale' refers to the sale, and 'day of taking possession' refers to the loan. In al-Bazzaziyya: hiring is like sale, and the debt is upon this; in marriage, he must pay the value of those dirhams; and if some of the price was paid and some not, the remainder becomes invalid. He said: (And if the fulus became obsolete after the loan, the like must be repaid, that is, in number according to Abu Hanifa. They both said: he must pay their value because it is impossible to return them as he received them, since what was received is a price and what is returned is not, so equivalency is lost, so it becomes like when one borrows a mithli (fungible) and then it becomes unavailable. But according to Abu Yusuf, he must pay the value on the day of taking possession; according to Muhammad, on the day of obsolecence. Muhammad's opinion is more considerate of the borrower because the value on the day of cutoff is lower. Similarly, regarding the lender's right, considering Abu Hanifa's opinion, Abu Yusuf's opinion is easier because the value on the day of taking possession is known, while on the day of obsolecence it is not known except with difficulty. For Abu Hanifa, the loan is an 'ariyah (borrowing for use) and its requirement is returning the substance in meaning, which is achieved by returning its like; the characteristic of being a price is additional. The dispute is based on the dispute in the case of someone who usurped a mithli like fresh dates and then it became unavailable among people; its value is due by consensus, but according to Abu Hanifa on the day of litigation, according to Abu Yusuf on the day of usurpation, and according to Muhammad on the day of cutoff. In al-Misbah: the fils that is used in transactions, its plural for few is aflus and for many is fulus. He said: (In Fath al-Qadir: And if he borrowed predominantly adulterated dirhams...) Regarding fulus: if he lent them and then they became obsolete, and in al-Bazzaziyya, similarly the dispute if he lent food in Iraq and took it in Mecca, then according to the Second (Abu Yusuf), he must pay its value on the day he took possession in Iraq; according to Muhammad, its value in Iraq on the day they disputed. Similarly, the dispute in usurped fulus if they become obsolete while the corporeal substance still exists, and similarly in 'adali. Then he said: If he bought with current currency and they took possession, then they revoked (taqayala) after obsolecence, the seller returns the like not the value according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa). If he bought with obsolete currency without specification and designation, the contract is invalid like the sudden obsolecence. They said: if it were a marriage, the mahr al-mithl would be due, but there is consideration. It should be said: if the value of the obsolete is ten or more, it is hers; if less, then the full ten. And if general obsolecence occurred in all regions, then it became current again before the sale was rescinded, the sale returns to being valid because the contract was not rescinded without rescission. He said: (And if he bought something with half a dirham of fulus, it is valid, and he must pay fulus that can be sold for half a dirham.) He said: (And if someone gave a money-changer a dirham...) Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage addresses the consequences when borrowed fulus become obsolete (kasād). The central dispute among Hanafi imams concerns whether the borrower must return the same number (like) or the value (qimah), and at what date the value is assessed. According to Abu Hanifa, the borrower must return the same number of fulus (mithl) because a loan (qard) is essentially a borrowing for use ('ariyah), where the obligation is to return the substance, which is fulfilled by returning the like. The fact that fulus function as price (thaman) is incidental. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) argue that the borrower must return the value, since the fulus received were a price, and returning the like when the fulus are obsolete would not achieve equivalency (mumathalah). They draw a qiyas to borrowing a fungible (mithli) that later becomes unavailable (inqita'), where consensus requires paying value. However, they differ on the valuation date: Abu Yusuf takes the day the borrower received the fulus (yawm al-qabd), Muhammad takes the day they became obsolete (yawm al-kasād), while in the usurpation analogy (ghasb), Abu Hanifa takes the day of litigation (yawm al-khusumah). The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's opinion, out of kindness to people (rifqan bi al-nas). The passage also discusses related scenarios: if the fulus rose or fell in price (ghala/rukhs), the like is returned by agreement—the dispute is specific to obsolecence. Further details include the case of predominantly adulterated dirhams (ghalibat al-ghish), where Abu Yusuf analogizes to Abu Hanifa's position but does not explicitly narrate it. The note from al-Bazzaziyya clarifies that the dispute extends to cases of 'cutoff' (inqita') similar to obsolecence. Additional sub-rulings: if a sale with current fulus is rescinded (taqayala) after obsolecence, the seller repays the like not the value; if a purchase with obsolete fulus without specification occurs, the contract is fasid (invalid). For general obsolecence followed by return to currency, the sale revives automatically. The final marginal note from Sharunbulaliyya points out an apparent problem in the money-changer scenario's wording, leaving it for further study.

Key Terms
الكساد (al-kasād): Obsolecence of currency; the state where fulus are no longer accepted as legal tender.
المثل (al-mithl): The like or equivalent in number/quantity; for fulus, it means the same number of coins regardless of their current status.
القيمة (al-qīmah): The monetary value of the fulus (in dirhams) calculated at a specific date as determined by ikhtilaf.
الغش (al-ghish): Adulteration in coinage; silver dirhams with a lower silver content than standard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what must a borrower repay when borrowed fulus become obsolete (kasād)?
A) The same number of fulus (like) regardless of their value.
B) The value of the fulus on the day they were borrowed.
C) The value of the fulus on the day they became obsolete.
D) The value of the fulus on the day of litigation.

Key Takeaway
When fulus become obsolete, the mufta bihi is repayment of their value on the day of obsolescence, based on Muhammad's view for kindness to people.

Mixed Currency Exchange: Flus and Fractional Silver
Ruling on mixed-currency exchange with flus and silver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mixed-exchange transaction involving flus and silver, and evaluate the effect of splitting the request according to the three Hanafi imams.

Core Idea
A single request for mixed currencies is valid; splitting the request invalidates the silver portion according to the Sahibayn.

Imagine giving a moneychanger a dirham and asking for half a dirham in flus and half dirham minus one habba in silver. Is this permissible? The Hanafi school says yes, provided the amounts are known and customary. But what if you phrase the request as two separate statements? The imams differ. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and explores the role of urf (custom) in validating such transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and its default ruling
The core transaction: giving a dirham and requesting half of it in flus and the other half minus a small amount (habba) in silver. According to the matn and commentary, this is valid.
This is the first scenario in the text.
2
Recognize the condition of urf
The validity hinges on urf (common practice): such mixed exchanges are customary and the amounts are known, thus no dispute arises.
The commentary explicitly states that urf makes the transaction free from ambiguity.
3
Examine three formulations of the request
The text distinguishes three phrasings: (1) 'Give me half dirham flus and half except a habba' (valid by consensus), (2) 'Give me with half of it flus and with half of it half except a habba' (invalid per Abu Hanifa, only silver invalid per Sahibayn), (3) 'Give me with half of it flus and give me with half of it half except a habba' (flus valid, silver invalid by consensus).
The key difference is whether the request is one statement or two, and whether the contract is divisible.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that splitting the statement splits the contract, and invalidity in one part spreads to the whole. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the contract can be divided, so only the problematic part (silver) is invalid.
This is based on the principle that the contract is repeated with the repetition of the word (Abu Hanifa) or with the detail of the price (Sahibayn).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn: the flus portion is valid, only the silver portion is invalid.
The commentator implies this is stronger because invalidity is confined to the problematic part.

كتاب الصرف
ومن أعطى صيرفياً درهماً وقال اعطني به نصف درهم فلوس ونصفاً إلا حبة صح.
في الفلوس إذا أقرضها ثم كسدت وفي البزازية وكذا الخلاف إن أقرضه طعاماً بالعراق وأخذه بمكة، فعند الثاني عليه قيمته يوم قبضه بالعراق، وعند محمد قيمته بالعراق يوم اختصما، وكذا الخلاف في الفلوس المغصوبة إذا كسدت حال قيام العين، وكذا العدالي. ثم قال : ولو اشترى بالنقد الرائج وتقابضا ثم تقايلا بعد كساده رد البائع المثل لا القيمة عند الإمام، ولو اشترى بالنقد الكاسد بلا إشارة وتعيين فالعقد فاسد كالكاسد الطارئ وقالوا: لو كان مكانه نكاح وجب مهر المثل وفيه نظر، ويجب أن يقال لو قيمة الكاسد عشرة أو أكثر فهي لها، وإن أقل فتمام العشرة ، وإن طرأ الكساد العام في كل الأقطار ثم راجت قبل فسخ البيع يعود البيع جائزاً لعدم انفساخ العقد بلا فسخ اهـ. فعلى هذا قول المصنف سابقاً بطل البيع أي انفسخ أن فسخه من له الدراهم لا مطلقاً اهـ.
قوله : (ولو اشترى شيئاً بنصف درهم فلوس صح وعليه فلوس تباع بنصف درهم وعلى هذا لو قال بثلث درهم أو بربعه أو بدائق فلوس أو بقيراط فلوس لأن التبايع بهذا الطريق متعارف في القليل معلوم بين الناس لا تفاوت فيه فلا يؤدي إلى النزاع. قيد بما دون الدرهم لأنه لو اشترى بدرهم فلوس لا يجوز عند محمد، أو بدرهمين فلوس لا يجوز عند محمد لعدم العرف، وجوزه أبو يوسف في الكل للعرف وهو الأصح؛ كذا في الكافي والمجتبى والدائق سدس درهم والقيراط نصف السدس قوله : (ومن أعطى صيرفيا درهماً فقال أعطني به نصف درهم فلوس ونصفاً إلا حبة صح لأن قابل الدرهم بنصف درهم فلوس وبنصف درهم إلا حبة من الفضة فيكون نصف درهم إلا حبة بمقابلة الفضة ونصف درهم وحبة بمقابلة الفلوس . قيد بقوله «به لأنه لو قال أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وبنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة بطل في الكل على قياس قوله وعندهما صح في الفلوس وبطل فيما قابل الفضة لأن الفساد هما عند التفصيل يتقدر بقدر المفسد ،وعنده يتعدى وأصله أن العقد يتكرر عنده بتكرار اللفظ ، وعندهما بتفصيل الثمن حتى لو قال أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وأعطني بنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة جاز في الفلوس وبطل في الفضة بالإجماع فهنا صور : الأولى مسألة الكتاب أعطني نصف درهم فلوس ونصفاً إلا حبة صح اتفاقاً. الثانية أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وبنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة فسد في الكل عنده، وفي الفضة فقط عندهما الثالثة أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وأعطني بنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة جاز في الفلوس فقط. ولم يذكر المصنف القبض قبل الافتراق للعلم به مما قدمه، وحاصله إن تفرقا قبل القبض فسد في النصف إلا حبة لكونه صرفاً لا في الفلوس لأنها بيع فيكفي قبض أحد البدلين، ولو لم يعطه الدرهم ولم يأخذ الفلوس حتى افترقا بطل في الكل للافتراق عن دين بدين وقدمنا شيئاً من أحكام الفلوس في باب الربا وفي هذا الباب. وإلى هنا ظهر إن الأموال ثلاثة : ثمن بكل حال وهو النقدان، صحبه الباء أولاً، قوبل بجنسه أو لا، ومبيع بكل حال كالثياب والدواب وثمن من وجه مبيع من وجه كالمثليات غير النقدين من المكيل والموزون، فإن كان معيناً في العقد كان مبيعاً وإلا وصحبه الباء وقوبل بمبيع فهو ثمن وثمن بالاصطلاح وهو سلعة في الأصل كالفلوس، فإن كانت رائجة فهي ثمن وإلا فسلعة. ومن حكم الثمن عدم اشتراط وجوده في ملك العاقد عند العقد ولا يبطل بهلاكه ويصح الاستبدال به في غير الصرف والسلم وحكم المبيع في الكل . . ومن حكمها وجوب التساوي عند المقابلة بالجنس في المقدرات إلى آخر ما قدمناه في باب الربا والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب.
خلافه
لأن قوله اعطني مساومة كلفظ بعني وبالمساومة لا ينعقد البيع فكيف يتكرر بتكراره، ولعل الوجه أن يقال تكرار اعطني يدل على أن مقصوده تفريق العقد فحمل على أنهما عقدا عقدين؛ كذا في شرح
المجمع .
Book of Currency Exchange. Whoever gives a moneychanger a dirham and says: 'Give me with it half a dirham of flus and half except one habba' is valid...
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq. It begins by stating a ruling: if someone gives a moneychanger a dirham and requests half a dirham worth of flus and half dirham except one habba of silver, the transaction is valid. The commentary then discusses various related issues: when flus are loaned and then become invalid, disputes between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad about valuation, and the effect of general or partial currency collapse. It then explains another ruling: buying something for half a dirham of flus is valid, and the buyer must pay flus that can be sold for half a dirham. This is based on common practice (urf). A dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf is noted: Muhammad disallows buying with a dirham of flus due to lack of urf, while Abu Yusuf allows it, and the latter is preferred. The commentary then returns to the first ruling and analyzes three scenarios: (1) the original case is valid by consensus; (2) saying 'give me with half of it flus and with half of it half except a habba' is invalid according to Abu Hanifa but only invalid in the silver portion according to the two disciples; (3) saying 'give me with half of it flus and give me with half of it half except a habba' makes the flus portion valid and the silver portion invalid by consensus. The passage concludes by categorizing wealth into three types: always a price (gold and silver), always a commodity (like cloth), and something that can be either (like flus: when current, they are a price; otherwise a commodity).

Key Terms
فلوس (fulus): Copper coins used as currency, which can be either a price (thaman) or a commodity depending on whether they are current.
حبة (habbah): A unit of weight; 1 habbah = 1/24 of a dirham in the Hanafi system (or 1/48 of a dirham in some contexts). Here it is used as a fraction of a dirham.
دائق (da'iq): One-sixth of a dirham.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where someone says to a moneychanger, 'Give me with half of this dirham flus and with half of it half dirham except a habba,' what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The entire transaction is invalid.
B) The flus portion is valid, the silver portion is invalid.
C) Both portions are valid.
D) The entire transaction is valid only if they separate before taking possession.

Key Takeaway
A single request for mixed currencies is valid; splitting the request invalidates the silver portion per the Sahibayn, and the relied-upon view preserves the flus portion.

Rulings on Exchange with Fulus and Classification of Wealth in Hanafi Fiqh
Concludes the chapter on Sarf with specific transaction rulings and a classification of monetary types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mixed exchange of silver and fulus and classify wealth into absolute price, absolute commodity, and intermediate.

Core Idea
The validity of a mixed exchange depends on the nature of the items: sarf for silver requires immediate possession, while fulus can be treated as a sale.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we encounter a detailed scenario of a mixed exchange involving silver dirhams and fulus (copper coins). The ruling distinguishes between the silver portion (subject to sarf rules) and the fulus portion (treated as a sale). Additionally, the text provides a fundamental Hanafi classification of wealth that determines whether an item is treated as price or commodity. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the laws of exchange and avoiding invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios of mixed exchange
The passage presents three cases: (1) 'Give me half as fulus and half as silver except a grain' – Abu Hanifa says the whole contract becomes invalid except the fulus; the Sahibayn say only the silver portion is invalid. (2) 'Give me half as fulus and half as a half except a grain' – valid only for fulus. (3) If they separate without any possession, the whole is invalid as debt for debt.
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
2
Understand the condition of possession (qabd) before separation
For the silver portion (sarf), possession before separation is required; otherwise the contract becomes fasid. For fulus, it is considered a sale, so possession of one countervalue suffices.
This is because sarf requires both countervalues to be possessed on the spot, while a sale can be deferred.
3
Recognize the classification of wealth into three categories
The text states: (1) Absolute price (thaman bi kulli hal) – gold and silver. (2) Absolute commodity (mabi' bi kulli hal) – non-fungibles like clothes. (3) Intermediate – fungibles (mithliyyat) other than gold/silver, which are price when accompanied by 'ba' and met with a commodity; otherwise commodity. Fulus is originally a commodity but becomes price when current.
This classification determines the rules of exchange, especially regarding possession and equality.
4
Apply the rulings of price and commodity
Price does not need to be owned at the time of contract, can be substituted except in sarf and salam, and is not invalidated by destruction. Commodity requires ownership and specific rules of equality when exchanged for same genus.
These rulings are derived from the classification and affect contract validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا حبة فسد في الكل عنده، وفي الفضة فقط عندهما الثالثة أعطني بنصفه فلوساً وأعطني بنصفه نصفاً إلا حبة جاز في الفلوس فقط. ولم يذكر المصنف القبض قبل الافتراق للعلم به مما قدمه، وحاصله إن تفرقا قبل القبض فسد في النصف إلا حبة لكونه صرفاً لا في الفلوس لأنها بيع فيكفي قبض أحد البدلين، ولو لم يعطه الدرهم ولم يأخذ الفلوس حتى افترقا بطل في الكل للافتراق عن دين بدين وقدمنا شيئاً من أحكام الفلوس في باب الربا وفي هذا الباب. وإلى هنا ظهر إن الأموال ثلاثة : ثمن بكل حال وهو النقدان، ومبيع بكل حال كالثياب والدواب وثمن من وجه مبيع من وجه كالمثليات غير النقدين من المكيل والموزون، فإن كان معيناً في العقد كان مبيعاً وإلا وصحبه الباء وقوبل بمبيع فهو ثمن وثمن بالاصطلاح وهو سلعة في الأصل كالفلوس، فإن كانت رائجة فهي ثمن وإلا فسلعة. ومن حكم الثمن عدم اشتراط وجوده في ملك العاقد عند العقد ولا يبطل بهلاكه ويصح الاستبدال به في غير الصرف والسلم وحكم المبيع في الكل . . ومن حكمها وجوب التساوي عند المقابلة بالجنس في المقدرات إلى آخر ما قدمناه في باب الربا والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب. خلافه لأن قوله اعطني مساومة كلفظ بعني وبالمساومة لا ينعقد البيع فكيف يتكرر بتكراره، ولعل الوجه أن يقال تكرار اعطني يدل على أن مقصوده تفريق العقد فحمل على أنهما عقدا عقدين؛ كذا في شرح المجمع .
Except for a grain, it becomes invalid entirely according to him (Abu Hanifa), and only in the silver according to them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The third scenario: 'Give me half of it as fulus and give me half of it as half except a grain'—it is valid only in the fulus. The author (al-Kanz) did not mention possession (qabd) before separation because it is known from what he previously stated. The summary: If they separate before possession, it becomes invalid in the half except a grain because it is sarf (currency exchange), not in the fulus because it is a sale, so possession of one of the two countervalues suffices. If he did not give him the dirham and did not take the fulus until they separated, the whole becomes invalid due to separation on a debt for a debt. We have already presented some rulings of fulus in the chapter of riba and in this chapter. And up to here, it has become clear that wealth is of three types: absolute price – which is the two metals (silver and gold); absolute commodity – like clothes and animals; and price from one aspect and commodity from another – like mithliyyat (fungibles) other than the two metals among measured and weighed items. If it is specified in the contract, it is a commodity; otherwise, if accompanied by the letter 'ba' and is met with a commodity, then it is a price. And by convention, that which is originally a commodity, like fulus, if they are current, they are price; otherwise, they are a commodity. Among the rulings of price is that its existence in the ownership of the contracting party is not required at the time of contract, and it is not invalidated by its destruction, and it is valid to substitute it in other than sarf and salam. And the ruling of commodity applies to all... And among their rulings is the obligation of equality when matched with the same genus in measured items, up to the end of what we presented in the chapter of riba. And Allah the Exalted knows best what is correct. A difference of opinion: Because his statement 'give me' is a negotiation like the word 'sell me', and by negotiation a sale is not concluded, so how can it be repeated by its repetition? The likely opinion is that the repetition of 'give me' indicates that his intention is to separate the contract, so it is treated as two contracts. As in Sharh al-Majma'.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter on Sarf (currency exchange) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It first presents a specific ruling: if someone says 'Give me half of this dirham as fulus (copper coins) and give me half of it as a half silver piece except a grain,' the contract is valid only for the fulus portion according to Abu Hanifa and both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The text then discusses the requirement of possession (qabd) before separation: in a sarf contract, both parties must take possession before parting, otherwise the contract becomes invalid for the silver portion. However, for the fulus, it is considered a sale, so possession of one countervalue suffices. The author reminds that previously mentioned rulings on fulus apply. Next, the passage provides a classic Hanafi classification of wealth: (1) absolute price (thaman bi kulli hal) – gold and silver coins; (2) absolute commodity (mabi' bi kulli hal) – non-monetary goods like clothes and animals; (3) intermediate (thaman min wajh, mabi' min wajh) – fungible items like measured and weighed goods other than gold and silver, which can be either price or commodity depending on context. Fulus (copper coins) are originally a commodity but function as price when they are current currency. The passage also lists two important rulings for price: it need not be owned at the time of contract, and it can be substituted (except in sarf and salam). Finally, a difference of opinion is noted regarding the word 'give me' (a'tini) – whether it constitutes a binding offer or mere negotiation. The preferred view is that repeating 'give me' indicates an intention to make separate contracts.

Key Terms
فلوس (fulus): Copper coins; originally a commodity but function as price when current in circulation.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the countervalue given in a sale. In the classification, gold and silver are absolute thaman; fulus are conditional thaman.
صرف (sarf): The exchange of one currency for another, either same genus or different; requires immediate possession according to the majority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling if two parties separate after the contract of sarf without taking possession of the gold/silver countervalues?
A) The contract remains valid and possession can be delayed.
B) The contract becomes invalid (fasid) only for the amount not possessed.
C) The entire contract becomes invalid (fasid) because separation before possession invalidates the exchange.
D) The contract becomes batil (void) and cannot be corrected.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of wealth into three types determines the rules of exchange, especially the requirement of possession and the distinction between sarf and sale.

Definition and Rulings of Ḍamān al-Darak
Definition of ḍamān al-darak and its procedural rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ḍamān al-darak, state the order of litigation when a sold property is reclaimed, and explain why the guarantor is not liable for improvements.

Core Idea
In ḍamān al-darak, the buyer must first sue the seller; only after liability is established can he also claim from the guarantor.

When a purchased property is reclaimed by its true owner, the buyer faces a loss. Ḍamān al-darak is a guarantee that gives the buyer recourse for the price. This lesson unpacks the definition of this term and the procedural rules for claiming from the guarantor versus the seller, including an intra-Hanafi difference.

Guided Path
1
Define ḍamān al-darak
Identify the linguistic root (darak: to catch up) and the terminological meaning: recourse for the price when the sold item is reclaimed.
The term focuses on the price, not on other losses like improvements.
2
Identify the default order of litigation
According to Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, the buyer must first sue the seller to establish the seller's liability for the price. Only after that can the buyer choose to claim from the seller or the guarantor.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abū Yūsuf holds that the buyer may sue the guarantor directly, without first suing the seller. Understand both views but follow the mufta bihi.
This difference only concerns the order of litigation, not the existence of liability.
4
Recognize the exception for a free person
If the sold item turns out to be a free human (sale void ab initio), the buyer may sue either the seller or the guarantor immediately, by consensus.
This is because the sale is fundamentally invalid, so the guarantee is triggered without further process.
5
Scope of the guarantee
The guarantor is only liable for the original price, not for any improvements (buildings, plants) added by the buyer. Such improvements are treated separately.
This clarifies that ḍamān al-darak is limited to monetary reimbursement of the price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبما يدركك في هذا البيع وهذا هو ضمان الدرك. والدرك لغة بفتحتين وسكون الراء اسم من أدركت الشيء ومنه ضمان الدرك؛ كذا في المصباح. واصطلاحاً الرجوع بالثمن عند استحقاق المبيع. وفي البزازية من آخر الدعوى في فصل الاستحقاق: وإن استحق المبيع وله كفيل بالدرك لا يرجع على الكفيل ما لم يجب على البائع فبعده هو بالخيار ولا يرجع على الكفيل بقيمة البناء والغرس ا هـ. وفي السراج الوهاج: فإذا استحق المبيع كان للمشتري أن يخاصم البائع أولاً فإذا ثبت عليه استحقاق المبيع كان له أن يأخذ الثمن من أيهما شاء، وليس له أن يخاصم الكفيل أولاً في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي يوسف أن له ذلك وأجمعوا أنه لو ظهر المبيع حراً كان له أن يخاصم أيهما شاء ا هـ.
His saying: (And regarding what will catch up with you in this sale) and this is ḍamān al-darak. Al-darak linguistically, with two fatḥas and a sukūn on the rā, is a noun from 'adraktu al-shay' (I caught up with something), and from it is ḍamān al-darak; thus in al-Miṣbāḥ. Terminologically, it is the recourse for the price when the sold item is reclaimed. And in al-Bazzāziyyah from the end of 'al-Daʿwā' in the section of 'al-Istiḥqāq': 'If the sold item is reclaimed and there is a guarantor for darak, the buyer does not have recourse against the guarantor until (the debt) becomes obligatory on the seller; after that he has a choice, and he does not have recourse against the guarantor for the value of buildings and plants.' And in al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj: 'When the sold item is reclaimed, the buyer may litigate with the seller first; when the reclamation of the sold item is established against him, he may take the price from whichever of them he wishes. However, he may not litigate with the guarantor first in the apparent narration; and from Abū Yūsuf that he may do so, and they all agreed that if the sold item turns out to be free, he may litigate with whichever he wishes.'
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage defines ḍamān al-darak, which is a type of kafalah where the guarantor (kafil) guarantees that if the sold property is reclaimed by a third party (e.g., the true owner), the buyer can get the price back from the seller. The key rule from al-Bazzāziyyah is that the buyer cannot sue the guarantor directly until the seller's liability is established; once established, the buyer can choose to sue either the seller or the guarantor. However, the guarantor is not liable for improvements like buildings and plants made by the buyer—only for the original price. Al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj adds a nuance: the buyer must first sue the seller; only after the seller's liability is proven can the buyer claim from either. This is the position of the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah. Abū Yūsuf allows suing the guarantor first. There is consensus that if the sold item is discovered to be a free person (meaning the sale is void ab initio), the buyer may sue either immediately. Note: The text says 'if the sold item is free' meaning a human being who is a free person, as selling a free person is invalid, so the guarantee of darak applies.

Key Terms
ضمان الدرك (ḍamān al-darak): A guarantee by which the guarantor undertakes to return the price to the buyer if the sold property is reclaimed by a rightful owner.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Reclamation of ownership by a third party who proves superior right to the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, whom must the buyer sue first when the sold property is reclaimed?
A) The guarantor of darak.
B) The seller.
C) Either the seller or the guarantor at his choice.
D) The original owner who reclaimed the property.

Key Takeaway
In ḍamān al-darak, the guarantor's liability follows the seller's; the buyer must first reclaim the price from the seller before turning to the guarantor.

Profit from Guarantor's Collection: Recommended Return vs. Ownership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on profit from guarantor's collected specific items.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three narrations of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn's position on profit from specific items collected by a guarantor, and explain why the mufta bihi is recommended return.

Core Idea
The guarantor's profit from collected specific items is tainted – return is recommended but not obligatory.

When a guarantor collects a debt in the form of specific goods (like wheat) and then makes a profit on them, does that profit belong to the guarantor or must it be returned to the debtor? The Hanafi school has multiple positions. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf and settles on the preferred ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the ruling
Recognize that the ruling concerns a guarantor (kafil) who collects a debt in the form of a specific, determinate item (shay' muta'ayyin) and then realizes a profit on it. The key terms: 'المقبوض المتعين' and 'ربح الكفيل'.
The debate only applies to specific items, not fungibles like money.
2
List the positions within the Hanafi school
Extract from the text the three narrations from Abu Hanifa: (1) return the profit to the debtor, (2) do not return it, (3) give it in charity. Also note the Sahibayn's position: the profit is the guarantor's.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
3
Understand the rationale (talil) behind each position
For Abu Hanifa: the profit has an element of khabath (legal impurity) because the debtor might have intended to pay his own debt, but the guarantor collected first. For the Sahibayn: the profit occurs in the guarantor's property, so it belongs to him without taint.
The impurity theory is unique to the specific-item scenario.
4
Identify which position is mufta bihi and why
According to al-Hidayah and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the most correct view is that the guarantor should return the profit (or give charity), but it is only recommended (mandub), not obligatory (wajib). The judge cannot compel the return.
'Asahh' indicates the preferred view, but compulsion is absent.
5
Distinguish from the case of a usurper (ghasib)
The text contrasts the guarantor with a usurper: a usurper who profits from a usurped specific item must return the profit and can be compelled because he has no right at all.
This distinction highlights that the guarantor has a genuine right to the debt due to his guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وندب رده على المطلوب لو شيئاً يتعين ) أي يستحب رد الربح على الأصيل إذا كان المقبوض شيئاً يتعين كالحنطة والشعير وهذا عند أبي حنيفة في رواية الجامع الصغير . له لا يرده وهو رواية عنه وعنه أنه يتصدق به . وقالا : هو لهما أنه ربح في ملكه له، وله أنه تمكن الخبث مع الملك إما لأنه بسبيل من الاسترداد بأن يقضيه بنفسه أو لأنه رضي به على اعتبار قضاء الكفيل، فإذا قضاه بنفسه لم يكن راضياً به وهذا الخبث يعمل فيما يتعين فيكون سبيله التصدق في رواية ويرده عليه في أخرى لأن الخبث لحقه، وهذا أصح لكنه استحباب لا جبر لأن الحق للكفيل ؛ كذا في الهداية وظاهر قوله «لا جبر» أن المراد بالاستحباب عدم جبر القاضي عليه وهو لا يستلزم عدم الوجوب فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى والتصدق به غير أنه ترجح الرد ؛ كذا في فتح القدير مختصراً. وقيد بما يتعين لأن ربح ما لا يتعين لا يندب رده على المطلوب. ولم يذكر المصنف رحمه الله تعالى أنه لا يطيب للأصيل إذا رده الكفيل أو لا، وحكمه كما في البناية أنه إذا كان الأصيل فقيراً طاب له وإن كان غنياً ففيه روايتان والأشبه كما قال فخر الإسلام في شرح الجامع الصغير إنه يطيب له لأنه إنما رده عليه لأنه حقه .اهـ. وقيد بالكفيل لأن الغاصب إذا ربح وجب رده على المالك ويجبر على الدفع له لأنه لا حق للغاصب في الربح ؛ كذا في البناية قوله: (وقيد بالكفيل لأن الغاصب الخ ) قال بعده في منح الغفار وفي فتح القدير أن الغاصب إذا أجر المغصوب ثم رده فإن الأجر له يتصدق به أو يرده إلى المغصوب منه اهـ. ولا مخالفة هذا وبين ما تقدم لأن بين ذلك في صورة ما إذا اتجر في المغصوب المتعين وربح فيه، وهذا فيما إذا أجر العين المغصوبة فإنه يملك الأجر بالعقد كما في الخانية والخلاصة وغيرهما من الكتب المعتمدة .اهـ.
His statement: 'and it is recommended to return it to the principal debtor if it is a specific thing' means it is recommended to return the profit to the original debtor if what was received is a specific thing like wheat and barley. This is according to Abu Hanifa in the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. In another narration from him, he does not return it, and in yet another, he gives it in charity. The two companions said: it is for the guarantor because for them the profit occurs in his property, while for him (Abu Hanifa) there is the presence of impurity along with ownership, either because it is subject to recovery by the debtor paying himself, or because he (the debtor) consented on the basis of the guarantor's payment, so if he pays himself, he was not consenting. This impurity operates in specific things, so its way is charity in one narration and return in another because the impurity pertains to him, and this is more correct, but it is recommended, not coercive, because the right belongs to the guarantor. This is from al-Hidayah. The apparent meaning of his statement 'not coercive' is that the recommendation means the judge does not compel him, which does not entail lack of obligation between him and Allah, and the charity is preferred. This is from Fath al-Qadir briefly. It is restricted to specific things because profit from non-specific things is not recommended to be returned. The author did not mention whether it becomes permissible for the original debtor if the guarantor returns it or not. Its ruling as in al-Binayah is that if the original debtor is poor, it is permissible for him; if rich, there are two narrations, the most similar as said by Fakhr al-Islam in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir is that it is permissible for him because he returned it to him because it is his right. It is restricted to the guarantor because if a usurper makes a profit, he must return it to the owner and is compelled to pay because the usurper has no right in the profit. This is from al-Binayah. His statement: 'Restricted to the guarantor because the usurper...' He said after it in Manh al-Ghaffar and in Fath al-Qadir that if the usurper leases the usurped property then returns it, the rent is for him; he gives it in charity or returns it to the usurped owner. There is no contradiction between this and what preceded because that is in the case when he trades with the specific usurped property and makes profit, while this is when he leases the usurped property, as he owns the rent by contract, as in al-Khaniyah, al-Khulasah, and other reliable books.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a guarantor who collects a debt from the principal debtor in the form of specific items (like wheat) and then makes a profit on them. The core issue is whether the profit belongs to the guarantor or must be returned to the debtor. The passage presents the positions of Abu Hanifa (in two narrations: one requires return, another charity), and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) who hold that the profit belongs to the guarantor. The preferred view is that the guarantor is recommended to return the profit, but not legally compelled, because the right to the profit is originally his. However, due to a sort of 'impurity' (khabath) arising from the possibility that the debtor might have paid himself, the profit should be disposed of either by return or charity. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that this applies only to specific goods; for non-specific goods (like money), no such recommendation exists. Additionally, the ruling is restricted to guarantors, distinguishing them from usurpers (ghasib) who must return any profit because they have no right in the usurped property. The passage also addresses the permissibility of the profit for the debtor if returned: it is permissible for a poor debtor but disputed for a rich one, with the preferred view that it is permissible.

Key Terms
الخبث (al-khabath): A legal impurity or taint that affects the permissibility of the profit due to potential conflict of rights.
الأصيل (al-asil): The original debtor who owes the debt, also called al-matlub.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The guarantor who stands surety for the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position, what should a guarantor do with profit earned from collected specific items?
A) Keep it for himself as it is his property.
B) Return it to the principal debtor, recommended but not compulsory.
C) Give it in charity as it is tainted.
D) Split it equally with the debtor.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor's profit from specific items is tainted; the mufta bihi is to return it as a recommendation, not a legal obligation.

Consent of the Transferee in Hawalah
Stating the relied-upon position that consent of the transferee is required in all cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi relied-upon position on consent in hawalah and apply the distinction between repugnant and non-repugnant claims made by the transferee.

Core Idea
Consent of the transferee is a non-negotiable condition for hawalah, regardless of his debtor status or debt type.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes the definitive position in the Hanafi school: the consent of the transferee (al-muhāl ʿalayh) is required for a valid hawalah, whether he owes a debt to the transferor or not, and whether the debt transferred matches his debt or not. He then expands into scenarios where, after a valid hawalah, the transferee makes claims to exonerate himself—illustrating a crucial principle: repugnant claims are not heard, but plausible ones are accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational ruling
Extract the core ruling: 'the relied-upon school is that the consent of the person transferred upon is necessary'. Note that this is stated unconditionally.
2
Examine the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies 'whether he owes a debt or not, and whether the transferred debt is similar to the debt or not'. Understand that no exception is made.
3
Analyze the subsidiary discussions
The author then considers situations after a valid hawalah where the transferee makes claims. Distinguish between two categories: claims that are 'repugnant' (like invalid marriage or illegal consideration) and claims that are 'not repugnant' (like payment, exoneration, or sale).
Repugnant claims contradict the basis of the hawalah or are inherently unlikely.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
Work through the examples from al-Bazzaziyyah (claim that debt was price of wine) and al-Karabisi (claim of invalid marriage vs. claims of payment/exoneration). Notice how the outcome changes.
5
Synthesize the principle
Conclude that a transferee's claim is heard only if it is plausible and not contradictory to the established transaction. The same principle applies to kafalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اهـ. والمذهب المعتمد أنه لا بد من رضا المحال عليه سواء كان عليه دين أو لا، وسواء كان المحال به مثل الدين أو لا . ثم اعلم أن الحوالة إذا صحت برضا المحال عليه وغاب المحيل فادعى المحال عليه ما يوجب براءة المحيل ليبرأ فهل تسمع دعواه؟ ففي البزازية : غاب المحيل وزعم المحتال عليه أن مال المحتال على المحيل كان ثمن خمر لا تصح دعواه وإن برهن على ذلك كما في الكفالة ا هـ . وفي فروق الكرابيسي : لو أحال امرأته بصداقها على رجل وقبل الحوالة ثم غاب الزوج فأقام المحتال عليه بينة أن نكاحها كان فاسداً لذلك وجهاً لا وبين تقبل بينته، ولو ادعى أنها كانت أبرأت زوجها عن صداقها أو أن الزوج أعطاها المهر أو باع بصداقها منها شيئاً وقبضت قبلت بينته، وإن كان المبيع غير مقبوض لا تقبل بينته، والفرق أن فساد النكاح متناقض أو لأنه يدعي أمراً مستنكراً فلا تسمع دعواه بخلاف دعوى الإبراء أو البيع لأنه غير مستنكر، وكذا هذا في الكفالة ا هـ . فعلى هذا لو ادعى المحيل أنه أوفاه الدين بعدها تسمع وتقبل بينته لأنه غير مستنكر.
End. And the relied-upon school is that the consent of the person transferred upon is necessary, whether he owes a debt or not, and whether the transferred debt is similar to the debt or not. Then know that if the hawalah is valid with the consent of the transferee, and the transferor is absent, and the transferee claims something that would exonerate the transferor to free himself [from liability], then is his claim heard? In al-Bazzaziyyah: The transferor was absent and the transferee claimed that the creditor's money due from the transferor was the price of wine; his claim is not accepted even if he brings proof, as in kafalah, end. And in Furuk al-Karabisi: If a man transferred his wife's dower to another man and she accepted the transfer, then the husband was absent, and the transferee brought proof that the marriage was invalid... there are two aspects: either his proof is accepted or not. And if he claimed that she had exonerated her husband from the dower, or that the husband had given her the dowry, or that she had bought something from him for her dower and took possession, his proof is accepted. But if the sold item was not taken possession, his proof is not accepted. The difference is that the invalidity of marriage is contradictory, or because he claims something repugnant, so his claim is not heard, unlike a claim of exoneration or sale because it is not repugnant. Likewise, this is in kafalah, end. Thus, if the transferor claims that he paid the debt after the transfer, it is heard and his proof is accepted because it is not repugnant.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with supporting references on the condition of consent in hawalah. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi madhhab is that the consent of the transferee (المحال عليه) is required for the validity of hawalah, regardless of whether he owes a debt to the transferor or not, and regardless of whether the debt transferred is similar in kind to the debt owed. The author then discusses a scenario where the hawalah is valid, the transferor is absent, and the transferee makes claims to exonerate himself, such as claiming that the underlying debt was invalid (e.g., price of wine). Citing al-Bazzaziyyah, such a claim is not accepted even with proof, as in kafalah. Then from Furuk al-Karabisi, a more detailed distinction is given: if a husband transfers his wife's dower, and the transferee claims the marriage was invalid, the claim is not accepted because it contradicts the basis of the hawalah. However, claims of exoneration, payment, or sale by the wife are accepted as plausible. The same principle applies in kafalah. Finally, if the transferor himself claims that he paid the debt after the hawalah, his claim is accepted because it is not repugnant.

Key Terms
المحال عليه (al-muhāl ʿalayh): The person upon whom the debt is transferred; the one who becomes the new debtor in a hawalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the claim of a transferee that the underlying debt was the price of wine?
A) The claim is accepted with proof.
B) The claim is not accepted even with proof.
C) The claim is accepted only if the transferor admits.
D) The claim is accepted only if the hawalah was for kafalah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon position requires the consent of the transferee for a valid hawalah, and claims that contradict the basis of the transaction are not accepted.

Return of Creditor Upon Insolvency of Transferee
Stating the rule of creditor reversion upon transferee insolvency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for the creditor's reversion in hawalah and identify the condition of insolvency and its exceptions.

Core Idea
The creditor returns to the transferor only upon the transferee's insolvency, unless an option was stipulated.

This lesson examines a key rule in hawalah (transfer of debt): when can the creditor abandon the transferee and pursue the original debtor? The Hanafi school establishes a general principle — no reversion except upon insolvency — but also recognizes exceptions when the contract includes an option. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly applying the law of debt transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and ruling
The act is the creditor's reversion to the transferor. The ruling is that it is prohibited except upon insolvency.
The general rule is 'لا يرجع إلا بالتوى'.
2
Analyze the condition for reversion
Insolvency (التوى) of the transferee is the sole condition that permits reversion without a stipulation.
This is the default; no reversion for any other reason.
3
Examine the exception to the rule
If the creditor was given the option (خيار) to revert at any time, or if the transfer was made on condition that he may pursue either party, then reversion is valid even without insolvency.
The exception is based on stipulation, not on the rule itself.
4
Understand the rationale
The transferor's exoneration is conditional upon the safety of the creditor's right. If the transferee becomes insolvent, the hawalah is effectively rescinded, similar to a defective sale.
The comparison to a sale (وصف السلامة في المبيع) clarifies the underlying principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولم يرجع المحتال على المحيل إلا بالتوى) لأن براءته مقيدة بسلامة حقه إذ هو المقصود، أو لفسخ الحوالة لفواته وأنها تحتمل الفسخ فصار كوصف السلامة في المبيع. وهذا إذا لم يشترط الخيار للمحال، أما إذا جعل للمحال الخيار أو أحاله على أن له أن يرجع على أيهما شاء صح ؛ كذا في البزازية. ومراده إذا كانت الحوالة باقية، أما إذا فسخت الحوالة فإن للمحتال الرجوع بدينه على المحيل ولذا قال في البدائع : إن حكمها ينتهي بفسخها وبالتوى. وفي البزازية والمحيل والمحتال يملكان النقض وبالنقض يبرأ المحتال عليه. وقدمنا عن الذخيرة أن الحوالة إذا تعددت على رجلين كانت الثانية نقضاً للأولى. وفيها أيضاً : قال محمد في الزيادات : رجل له على رجل ألف درهم وبها كفيل وعلى
His statement: (And the creditor does not return to the transferor except upon insolvency) because his exoneration is conditioned upon the safety of his right, for that is the objective, or because hawalah is rescinded due to its loss, and it is susceptible to rescission, so it becomes like the condition of safety in a sale. This is when the option of recourse was not stipulated for the creditor. But if the creditor was given the option, or he transferred on condition that he may return to either of them as he wishes, then it is valid. This is in al-Bazzaziyyah. And what is meant is when the hawalah remains effective. However, if the hawalah is rescinded, then the creditor has the right to return to the transferor with his debt. That is why it is said in al-Bada'i': its ruling ends with its rescission and with insolvency. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: both the transferor and the creditor have the power to annul; by annulment the transferee is exonerated. And we have previously cited from al-Dhakhirah that if hawalah is made successively on two men, the second constitutes an annulment of the first. And also in it: Muhammad said in al-Ziyadat: A man has one thousand dirhams due from another, and he has a guarantor for it, and...
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage states the rule that the creditor (المحتال) cannot revert to the original debtor (المحيل) unless the transferee becomes insolvent (التوى). The rationale is that the transferor's exoneration is conditional upon the preservation of the creditor's right. Alternatively, the hawalah itself is rescinded when the transferee fails to pay, as the purpose of the contract is lost. This is compared to the implied condition of safety in a sale (if the item is defective, the sale can be rescinded). The rule applies when no option of recourse was stipulated. If the creditor was given the option to return to the transferor at any time, or if the transfer was made on condition that the creditor may pursue either party, then it is valid to return even without insolvency. The author notes that if the hawalah is explicitly rescinded, the creditor also has the right to revert. Additionally, if a person makes a hawalah on two different individuals consecutively, the second hawalah effectively rescinds the first, as per al-Dhakhirah. The passage ends with a partial narration from al-Ziyadat regarding a debt with a guarantor.

Key Terms
التوى (al-tawā): Insolvency of the transferee; inability to pay the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can the creditor (المحتال) return to the original debtor (المحيل) in a hawalah?
A) Whenever the creditor wishes.
B) Only if the transferee becomes insolvent.
C) Only if both the transferor and creditor agree.
D) The creditor can never return to the transferor.

Key Takeaway
The creditor may only revert to the transferor upon the transferee's insolvency, unless an option was stipulated.
