Hawalah on Mukatab and on Price of Sold Slave
Dispute about effect of supervening events on hawalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule on the validity of hawalah when the underlying debt is extinguished after transfer, and distinguish between supervening events and non-existence of debt.

Core Idea
A hawalah remains valid if the debt existed at transfer and later falls by a supervening event; it is void only if the debt never existed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a critical nuance in the law of hawalah (transfer of debt). The question is whether a hawalah is voided when the debt is extinguished after the transfer due to an event like death of the subject matter or return of the sold item. The Hanafi school holds that such supervening events do not void the hawalah, contrary to Zufar. Another scenario—where the debt is reclaimed by a third party or the item turns out to be free—voids the hawalah by consensus because the debt never existed at the time of transfer. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying hawalah correctly in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of debt extinguishment
The passage distinguishes between debts that fall after valid existence (e.g., death of slave, return by option, emancipation of mukatab) and debts that are discovered to have never existed (e.g., istihqaq, slave turns out free). Identify which cases fall into each category.
For supervening events, the debt was valid at the time of hawalah.
2
Analyze the Hanafi rule for supervening events
According to the Hanafis, if the debt falls after it was validly owed, the hawalah is not voided. The passage gives examples: master transfers creditor to mukatab then emancipates him; seller transfers creditor to buyer for price then the slave dies or is returned. In all these, the hawalah remains valid.
The ruling is 'sahih' (valid) for these cases.
3
Examine the dispute with Zufar
Zufar disagreed on the mukatab case. He held that emancipation voids the hawalah because the debt is extinguished. The Hanafi response: the debt fell by a supervening event after existence, so the hawalah stands.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf involving Imam Zufar.
4
Distinguish the cases of istihqaq and appearance of freedom
If the sold item is reclaimed by a third party or the slave is found free, the price debt is void ab initio. Consequently, the hawalah is voided by consensus because the debt never existed at the time of transfer.
These are not supervening events but discoveries of non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو أحال المولى غريمه على المكاتب ببدل الكتابة ثم أعتق المولى المكاتب لم تبطل الحوالة عندنا خلافاً لزفر. وعلى هذا الخلاف ما إذا باع عبداً من رجل بألف درهم ثم إن البائع أحال غريماً بالثمن على المشتري فمات العبد قبل القبض أو رد بخيار من الخيارات الثلاث قبل القبض أو بعده لم تبطل، ولو استحق المبيع أو استحق الدين الذي قيد به الحوالة من جهة الغرماء أو ظهر أن العبد المبيع كان حراً بطلت الحوالة إجماعاً. والفرق أن في الأول سقط الدين بعد الوجوب مقصوداً فلم تبطل الحوالة، وفي الثاني ظهر عدم الوجوب وقت الحوالة فبطلت وإذا لم تبطل وأدى فإنه يرجع به على المحيل فيرجع المكاتب على سيده إن أداه بعد عتقه لا قبله كذا في الذخيرة. ثم قال : وفي المنتقى : رجل اشترى عبداً بألف درهم وقبضه ثم أحال المشتري البائع بالثمن على غريمه من المال الذي له عليه ثم رد المشتري العبد بعيب بقضاء فإن القاضي يبطل الحوالة، فإن كان البائع أجل المحتال عليه بالمال فإن الأجل ينتقض أيضاً إذا كان الرد بحكم، فإن كان الرد بغير حكم لا يبطل الأجل والمشتري بالخيار إن شاء اتبع البائع به حالاً وإن شاء اتبع المحتال عليه إلى أجله اهـ. فقد فرق على رواية المنتقى بين إحالة البائع غريمه على المشتري وبين إحالة المشتري البائع على غريمه حيث لا تبطل في الأولى بالفسخ، وتبطل في الثانية، ولعل وجهه المقيدة كما أفاده ما قررناه وفي ذلك مخالفة لما نقلناه عن البزازية فليتأمل. قوله: (والفرق إن في الأول) أي في إعتاق المكاتب وموت العبد المبيع أو رده بخيار لكن يرد عليه أن سقوط الدين بموت العبد ليس مقصوداً فالمناسب أن يقول إن الدين في الأول سقط بأمر عارض كما في الجوهرة حيث قال : وأما إن سقط الدين الذي قيدت به الحوالة بأمر عارض ولم تتبين براءة الأصيل منه لا تبطل الحوالة مثل أن يحتال بألف من ثمن مبيع فهلك المبيع قبل تسليمه على المشتري سقط الثمن عنه ولا تبطل الحوالة، ولكنه إذا أدى رجع على المحيل بما أدى لأنه قضى دينه بأمره اهـ. قوله: (ولعل وجهه أي وجه الفرق بينهما أنه في أن لا الأولى تبين دين عليه أي على المحال عليه وهو المشتري وهي تصح بدون دين عليه وفيه نظر، لأن ذلك في المطلقة وكلامنا في المقيدة فالمناسب أن يقول كما قدمناه عن الجوهرة أن في الأولى تبين سقوط الدين بأمر عارض وهو الفسخ بالعيب. قوله : (وفي
So if the master transfers his creditor to the mukatab for the kitabah payment, then the master emancipates the mukatab, the hawalah is not voided according to us, contrary to Zufar. And upon this disagreement is if a person sold a slave to a man for one thousand dirhams, then the seller transfers a creditor to the buyer for the price, then the slave dies before possession or is returned by one of the three options before or after possession, it is not voided. And if the sold item is rightfully reclaimed (istihqaq) or the debt by which the hawalah was restricted is reclaimed from the creditors, or it appears that the sold slave was free, the hawalah is voided by consensus. The difference is that in the first case the debt fell after its obligation intentionally, so the hawalah was not voided; in the second case it appears that there was no obligation at the time of hawalah, so it was voided. And if it is not voided and he pays, then he returns to the muheel; the mukatab returns to his master if he paid after emancipation, not before. This is in al-Dhakhira. Then he said: In al-Muntaqa: A man bought a slave for one thousand dirhams and took possession, then the buyer transferred the seller to his creditor for the money owed to him, then the buyer returned the slave due to a defect by a court order; the judge voids the hawalah. If the seller had given a term to the muhal 'alayh for the money, the term is also annulled if the return was by judgment; if the return was without judgment, the term is not annulled, and the buyer has the option: if he wishes he can pursue the seller immediately, or he can pursue the muhal 'alayh until his term. So a distinction was made according to the transmission of al-Muntaqa between the seller transferring his creditor to the buyer and the buyer transferring the seller to his creditor: the former is not voided by rescission, while the latter is voided. Perhaps its reason is the restricted nature as our explanation shows, and in that there is a contradiction to what we transmitted from al-Bazzaziyya, so let it be reflected. His saying: 'and the difference is that in the first' i.e., in the emancipation of the mukatab and the death of the sold slave or its return by option, but it is objected that the falling of the debt by the death of the slave is not intentional, so it is appropriate to say that the debt in the first fell by a supervening event, as in al-Jawhara where he said: As for if the debt by which the hawalah was restricted falls by a supervening event and the original debtor's discharge is not evident, the hawalah is not voided, like if one transfers one thousand dirhams from the price of a sale, then the sold item perishes before delivery to the buyer, the price falls from him and the hawalah is not voided, but when he pays, he returns to the muheel for what he paid because he paid his debt by his order. His saying: 'perhaps its reason' i.e., the reason for the distinction between them is that in the first, a debt is evident against the muhal 'alayh (the buyer), and it is valid without a debt against him, and there is a consideration because that is in unrestricted, and our talk is in restricted, so it is appropriate to say as we presented from al-Jawhara that in the first, the falling of the debt by a supervening event is evident, which is rescission due to defect. His saying: 'And in'
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage examines the effect of the underlying debt being extinguished after the hawalah. It begins with the example of a master who transfers his creditor to his mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) for the kitabah payment. If the master then emancipates the mukatab, the hawalah is not voided according to the Hanafis, contrary to Zufar. Similarly, if a seller transfers a creditor to the buyer for the price of a sold slave, and then the slave dies before delivery or is returned due to a defect or option, the hawalah is not voided. However, if the sold item is reclaimed (istihqaq) or if the debt itself is reclaimed from the muheel's creditors, or if the slave turns out to be free, the hawalah is voided by consensus. The key distinction: in the first set of cases, the debt falls after it was validly owed (by a supervening event like death or return), so the hawalah remains valid; in the second set, it is discovered that the debt never existed at the time of hawalah, so it is voided. The passage then quotes from al-Muntaqa a case where a buyer resells the price debt to the seller's creditor; in that case, the hawalah is voided because the debt was not owed by the muhal 'alayh at the time. The author notes a potential contradiction with al-Bazzaziyya and calls for reflection. The grammatical commentary at the end clarifies that the first cases involve a debt that fell by a supervening event (like death or return) and that the hawalah is not voided.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn and pay a sum for his freedom. The kitabah payment is the consideration.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A legal claim that invalidates ownership, such as a third party proving the sold item was theirs.
الحوالة (al-hawalah): Transfer of debt from one debtor to another with the creditor's consent.
المحيل (al-muheel): The original debtor who transfers the debt to another person.
المحتال عليه (al-muhal 'alayh): The person to whom the debt is transferred; the new debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a seller transfers his creditor to the buyer for the price of a slave, and then the slave dies before delivery, what happens to the hawalah?
A) The hawalah is voided because the price debt is gone.
B) The hawalah remains valid because the debt existed at the time of transfer.
C) The hawalah becomes void only if the buyer returns the slave.
D) The hawalah is voided by the death of the slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a hawalah is not voided by supervening events that extinguish the debt after it was validly owed; it is only voided if the debt never existed at the time of transfer.

6 min
The Two Scenarios of Hawala Validity
Ruling on hawala validity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios of hawala validity and explain why hawala is valid without a debt on the assignee but requires a debt on the assignor.

Core Idea
Hawala is valid if the assignee owes nothing but invalid if the assignor owes nothing.

This passage distinguishes two key cases in hawala (assignment of debt). The first shows hawala is valid even if the assignee (muḥāl ʿalayhi) does not owe the assignor; the second shows hawala is invalid if the assignor (muḥīl) is not a debtor. This distinction is fundamental to understanding the conditions of hawala in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Locate where the text describes the first scenario (no debt on assignee) and the second scenario (no debtor status of assignor). Note the ruling for each.
The first scenario: 'تبين أن لا دين عليه وهي تصح بدون دين على المحال عليه' (it becomes clear that there is no debt upon him and it is valid without a debt on the assignee). The second: 'ظهر أن المحيل ليس بمديون فبطلت' (it appears that the assignor is not a debtor, so it becomes invalid).
2
Understand the rulings
For the first scenario, hawala is valid because a debt on the assignee is not a condition. For the second, hawala is invalid because the assignor's debt is essential.
This aligns with Hanafi usul: hawala is a transfer of a debt from the assignor's liability, so the assignor must be indebted.
3
Apply to a simple case
A owes B $100. B assigns this debt to C. C does not owe B anything. Is the hawala valid? (Yes, because C's lack of debt does not affect validity.) If it turns out A is not a debtor, is the hawala valid? (No, because the basis of hawala is the assignor's debt.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن في الأولى تبين أن لا دين عليه وهي تصح بدون دين على المحال عليه، وفي الثانية ظهر أن المحيل ليس بمديون فبطلت ثم قال في الذخيرة وإن كان البائع أبرأ المحتال عليه من المال أو وهبه له أو اشترى منه ثوباً وقبضه ثم رد المشتري المبيع بعيب بقضاء أو بغيره جازت الهبة والإبراء والبائع ضامن للمال وكذا لو مات العبد في يد البائع قبل القبض، وكذا لو استحق بعده وقد أبرأ البائع المحتال عليه من المال أو وهبه له .اهـ. وهو مشكل بالنسبة إلى مسألة الاستحقاق لما تقدم من بطلان الحوالة إذا استحق المبيع لأنه تبين أن لا دین أصلاً فلما بطلت ينبغي أن يبطل ما ابتنى عليها من الهبة والإبراء من البائع وقد وقعت حادثة الفتوى في المديون إذا باع شيئاً من دائنه بمثل الدين ثم أحال عليه بنظير الثمن أو بالثمن فهل تصح أو لا؟ فأجبت إذا وقع بنظيره صحت لأنها لم تقيد بالثمن ولا يشترط لصحتها دين على المحال عليه، وإن وقعت بالثمن فهي مقيدة بالدين وهو مستحق للمحال عليه لوقوع المقاصة بنفس الشراء، وقدمنا أن الدين إذا استحق للغير فإنها تبطل والله أعلم.
مر
الثانية ظهر أن المحيل ليس بمديون فبطلت قال بعض الفضلاء لا يظهر لأن الحوالة تصح بدون دين على المحيل أيضاً كما . متناً، وكان الظاهر أن يقول وفي الثانية ظهر أن الحوالة بمعنى الوكالة وللوكيل الامتناع عنها اهـ. فتأمل قوله : (وهو مشكل الخ قد يجاب بأن المحتال وهو البائع قد المحتال عليه بإبرائه أو هبته قبضاً حكمياً وبالشراء منه صار قابضاً قبضاً حقيقياً، وقد من علمت أن هذه الحوالة بمعنى الوكالة فصار البائع كالوكيل عن المشتري فيرجع عليه بما قبضه بعد بطلان الحوالة تأمل .
That in the first case it becomes clear that there is no debt upon him, and it (the hawala) is valid without a debt on the assignee. In the second case, it appears that the assignor is not a debtor, so it (the hawala) becomes invalid. Then he said in al-Dhakhira: If the seller releases the assignee from the debt or gives it to him as a gift, or buys a garment from him and takes possession, then the buyer returns the sold item due to a defect by court order or otherwise, the gift and release are valid, and the seller is liable for the debt. Likewise if the slave dies in the seller's hand before possession, or if it is reclaimed after, and the seller has released the assignee from the debt or given it as a gift. End quote. This is problematic regarding the case of reclamation because of what preceded that the hawala is invalid if the sold item is reclaimed, as it becomes clear that there was no debt at all. So when it becomes invalid, what was built upon it from the gift and release by the seller should also become invalid. A fatwa incident occurred: if a debtor sells something to his creditor for an amount equal to the debt, then assigns the creditor onto the buyer in exchange for the price or for the price itself: is it valid or not? I answered: if it occurs in exchange for its equivalent, it is valid because it is not qualified by the price, and for its validity a debt on the assignee is not required. If it occurs for the price, then it is qualified by the debt, and the debt is due to the assignee because of the set-off occurring by the very purchase. We have previously stated that if the debt becomes due to someone else, the hawala becomes invalid. And Allah knows best.

It has passed.

The second: It appears that the assignor is not a debtor, so it becomes invalid. Some virtuous person said: It does not appear, because the hawala is valid without a debt on the assignor as well, as in the text. It should have been said: In the second, it appears that the hawala is in the sense of agency, and the agent has the right to refrain from it. End quote. So reflect. His statement: 'It is problematic...' may be answered by saying that the assignee, who is the seller, has taken from the assignee (the debtor) by his release or gift a constructive possession, and by the purchase he becomes a real possessor. And you have known that this hawala is in the sense of agency, so the seller becomes like an agent for the buyer, so he can claim from him what he took after the invalidity of the hawala. Reflect.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-discussion about the validity and invalidity of hawala (assignment of debt) in cases where the underlying debt does not exist or the assignor turns out not to be a debtor. The author first distinguishes two scenarios: (1) the assignee has no debt on him (but the hawala is still valid because a debt on the assignee is not required), and (2) the assignor is not a debtor, which invalidates the hawala. He then quotes al-Dhakhira regarding cases where the seller (assignee) releases or gifts the debt, or buys an item and later returns it; these actions are valid and the seller remains liable. This creates a problem with the rule that hawala is invalid if the sold item is reclaimed (because it shows no debt existed). The author resolves this by explaining that the seller's actions constitute constructive or actual possession, so when hawala is invalidated, the seller acts as the buyer's agent and can recover what he paid. Finally, a fatwa incident is discussed: if a debtor sells his own property to his creditor for the exact debt amount and then assigns the creditor onto the buyer, the ruling depends on whether the hawala is for the equivalent or the actual price.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Assignment of debt; a transfer of a debt from the assignor (muḥīl) to the assignee (muḥāl ʿalayh), who becomes the new debtor to the assignee (muḥtāl).
المحال عليه (al-muḥāl ʿalayhi): The person on whom the debt is assigned; the new debtor who now owes the assignee.
المحتال (al-muḥtāl): The creditor who accepts the assignment; the person to whom the debt is assigned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on hawala if the assignor (muḥīl) is not a debtor?
A) The hawala is valid because hawala does not require a debt on the assignor.
B) The hawala is invalid because the assignor's debt is essential.
C) The hawala becomes a gift and is valid.
D) The hawala is valid if the assignee accepts.

Key Takeaway
Hawala requires the assignor to be a debtor but does not require the assignee to be indebted.

Fatwa on Sale to Creditor and Assignment
Fatwa on hawala after sale to creditor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two types of assignment (hawala) after a sale to a creditor and determine the validity based on whether the assignment is for the equivalent or the actual price.

Core Idea
Assignment for the equivalent of the debt is valid; assignment for the price is tied to the debt and may be invalid due to set-off.

This passage presents a fatwa (legal opinion) about a common scenario: a debtor sells his property to his creditor for the exact amount of the debt, and then assigns the creditor onto a third party. The ruling depends on whether the assignment is made for the equivalent (without specifying the price) or for the price itself. This distinction has practical implications for debt resolution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fatwa scenario
A debtor (assignor) sells something to his creditor for an amount equal to the existing debt. Then he assigns the creditor (assignee, muhtal) onto a buyer (assignee, muhtal alayh) in exchange for either the equivalent of the debt or for the actual price.
The sale and assignment are separate contracts.
2
Identify the two forms of assignment
The text distinguishes: assignment (hawala) 'for its equivalent' (binazirihi) vs assignment 'for the price' (bil-thaman).
The phrase 'بنظير الثمن' means for the equivalent of the price/debt; 'بالثمن' means for the price itself.
3
Determine validity based on condition
If the assignment is for the equivalent, it is valid because it is not tied to the debt; the assignee owes nothing to the assignor originally, so no set-off occurs. If for the price, it is tied to the debt, and since the purchase creates a set-off (muqasah), the debt is already discharged, making the assignor not a debtor and thus the hawala invalid.
The key: set-off occurs at the moment of sale, so the debt is extinguished; subsequent assignment for the price is invalid because there is no debt to transfer.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a case: A owes B $100. A sells his watch to B for $100. Then A assigns B (creditor) onto C for $100. If the assignment is stated as 'for the equivalent of the debt', it is valid; if stated as 'for the price', it is invalid because the debt was set-off by the purchase.

Source Text

Types and Rulings of Bribery in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines bribery and enumerates its four types with rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of bribery and apply the correct ruling for each party (giver/taker) in classical Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Bribery is haram, but the taker's and giver's rulings differ by type; the taker is always prohibited unless it is a merited reward or a lawful hire.

Bribery (ar-rishwah) is a pervasive issue with nuanced rulings in Hanafi fiqh. This lesson unpacks its definition, contrasts it with gifts, and examines four distinct types from the Hanafi tradition, clarifying which transactions are absolutely forbidden and which have exceptions. Understanding these categories prevents applying overly broad prohibitions and equips you to evaluate real-world scenarios with precision.

Guided Path
1
Define bribery and differentiate from a gift
Identify the definition of rishwah as a payment conditional on help, contrasting it with hadiyyah (unconditional gift). Note the linguistic point: 'rashwa' with kasr is correct; opening the bā' is colloquial.
This distinction is foundational; many errors arise from conflating gifts with bribes.
2
Enumerate the four types of bribery from al-Khāniyyah
List the four categories: (1) haram from both sides (judgeship through bribery, bribe for a judgment), (2) haram for taker only (due to fear/duress), (3) permissible for giver but not taker (to arrange matters with ruler, unless using hire), (4) permissible from both sides (gifts for friendship).
This taxonomy is crucial as each type has a different legal effect.
3
Analyze each type's ruling for giver and taker
For each type, state the hukm (haram/permissible) for giver and taker separately, citing the conditions (e.g., duress, prior condition). Note that the text also discusses after-service reward and special cases like poets and lovers.
Pay special attention to the distinction between bribery given before vs. after the service is rendered.
4
Evaluate the legal stratagem (hire) to make bribery permissible for the taker
Understand that if the giver wants the taker to take lawfully, he can hire the taker for a day with the payment as wages. This hire contract is valid and makes the payment permissible as wages.
This is a practical hila (legal device) recognized in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on reward after service without prior condition
Some say it is impermissible for the taker; the correct view (as per the text) is that it is permissible because it is a reward for kindness (mujazat al-ihsan). Recognize the basis and the tarjih.
The text states 'that is correct' for the permissive view; this is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بكسر الباء الرشوة وفي المثل البراطيل تنصر الأباطيل كناية مأخوذ من البرطيل الذي هو المعول لأنه يستخرج به ما استتر وفتح الباء عامي لفقد فعليل بالفتح ا هـ. وذكر الأقطع أن الفرق بين الهدية والرشوة أن الرشوة ما يعطيه بشرط أن يعينه والهدية لا شرط معها اه. وفي الخانية: الرشوة على وجوه أربعة منها ما هو حرام من الجانبين وذلك في موضعين: أحدهما إذا تقلد القضاء بالرشوة حرم على القاضي والآخذ. وفي صلح المعراج : تجوز المصانعة للأوصياء في أموال اليتامى وبه يفتى. ثم قال : من الرشوة المحرمة على الآخذ دون الدافع ما يأخذه الشاعر. وفي وصايا الخانية : قالوا بذل المال لاستخلاص حق له على آخر رشوة. الثاني إذا دفع الرشوة إلى القاضي ليقضي له حرم من الجانبين، سواء كان القضاء بحق أو بغير حق. ومنها إذا دفع الرشوة خوفاً على نفسه أو ماله فهو حرام على الآخذ غير حرام على الدافع، وكذا إذا طمع في ماله فرشاه ببعض المال ومنها إذا دفع الرشوة ليسوي أمره عند السلطان حل له الدفع ولا يحل للآخذ أن يأخذ، فإن أراد أن يحل للآخذ يستأجر الآخذ يوماً إلى الليل بما يريد أن يدفع إليه فإنه تصح هذه الإجارة، ثم المستأجر إن شاء استعمله في هذا العمل وإن شاء استعمله في غيره. هذا إذا أعطاه الرشوة أولاً ليسوي أمره عند السلطان، وإن طلب منه أن يسوي أمره ولم يذكر له الرشوة وأعطاه بعدما يسوي اختلفوا فيه، قال بعضهم : لا يحل له أن يأخذ. وقال بعضهم: يحل وهو الصحيح لأنه يريد مجازاة الإحسان فيحل ا هـ . ولم أر قسماً يحل الأخذ فيه دون الدفع وأما الحلال من الجانبين فهو الإهداء للتودد والمحبة كما صرحوا به وليس هو من الرشوة لما علمت. وفي القنية قبيل التحري : الظلمة تمنع الناس من الاحتطاب من المروج إلا بدفع شيء اليهم فالدفع والأخذ حرام لأنه رشوة .ا.هـ. وفيها ما يدفعه المتعاشيقان رشوة يجب ردها ولا تملك ا هـ . فهذا يفيد أن الآخذ لا يملكها وقد صرح به في هبة القنية قال : وفي السير الكبير : الرشوة لا تملك إلى أن قال : أبرأه عن الدين ليصلح مهمه عند السلطان لا يبرأ وهو رشوة، ولو أبى الاضطجاع عند امرأته فقال أبرئيني عن المهر فأضطجع معك فأبرأته قيل يبرأ لأن الإبراء للتودد الداعي للجماع وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام تهادوا تحابوا بخلاف الإبراء في الأول لأنه مقصود على اصلاح المهم واصلاح المهم مستحق عليه ديانة، وبذل المال فيما هو مستحق عليه حد الرشوة وفيها : دفع للقاضي أو لغيره سحتاً لإصلاح المهم فأصلح ثم ندم يرد ما دفع إليه ا هـ . فظاهر أن التوبة من الرشوة برد المال إلى صاحبه وإن قضى حاجته. وفي صلح المعراج تجوز اه. (وفي صلح المعراج إلى قوله الثاني كذا وجد في بعض النسخ وفي بعضها «كتب» قبل قوله الآتي وليس منه ما تأخذه المرأة وهو محله» .
With kasr of the ba' is 'ar-rishwah' (bribery). In the proverb: 'al-barāṭīl (bribes) support falsehoods' — a metaphor derived from al-barṭīl which is the pickaxe because it extracts what is hidden. Opening the ba' is colloquial due to the lack of fa'īl with fatha. Al-Aqta' mentioned that the difference between a gift and a bribe is that a bribe is given with the condition that he helps him, while a gift has no condition with it. In al-Khāniyyah: Bribery is of four types: Among them is that which is forbidden from both sides, and that occurs in two places: one of them is if he assumes the judgeship through bribery, then it is forbidden for the judge and the taker. In Sulh al-Mi'rāj: It is permissible to give compensation (muṣāna‘ah) to guardians over the wealth of orphans, and according to this the fatwa is given. Then he said: Among the bribery that is forbidden for the taker but not the giver is what a poet takes. In Waṣāyā al-Khāniyyah: They said: Spending wealth to extract a right he has against another is bribery. Second: If he gives a bribe to the judge to rule in his favor, it is forbidden from both sides, whether the ruling is with right or without right. Among them: If he gives a bribe out of fear for his life or property, it is forbidden for the taker, not forbidden for the giver. Similarly, if he covets his wealth and bribes him with some of the wealth. Among them: If he gives a bribe to arrange his matter with the sultan, it is permissible for him to give, but it is not permissible for the taker to take. If he wants it to be permissible for the taker, he hires the taker for a day until night with what he intends to give him, then this hire contract is valid. Then the hirer, if he wishes, he employs him in this work, and if he wishes, he employs him in other work. This is if he gave the bribe first to arrange his matter with the sultan. If he asked him to arrange his matter and did not mention the bribe to him, and gave it to him after he arranged it, there is disagreement. Some said: It is not permissible for him to take. Some said: It is permissible, and that is correct because he intends to reciprocate the kindness, so it is permissible. I have not seen a category where it is permissible for the taker to take but not the giver. As for what is permissible from both sides, it is giving as a gift for friendship and love, as they explained, and it is not from bribery as you have learned. In al-Qunyah, before al-Taḥarrī: The oppressors prevent people from collecting firewood from meadows except with payment of something to them, so the payment and taking are forbidden because it is bribery. In it: What the two lovers give is bribery; it must be returned and is not owned. This indicates that the taker does not own it, and it is clearly stated in Hibat al-Qunyah. He said: In al-Siyar al-Kabīr: Bribery is not owned. Until he said: He released him from debt to arrange his matter with the sultan — he is not released, and it is bribery. If he refused to lie down with his wife and she said: Release me from the dower so I lie down with you, and she released him, it is said he is released because the release is for the friendship that invites intercourse, and the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Give gifts to each other, you will love each other.' Unlike the release in the first case because it was intended for arranging the matter, and arranging the matter is incumbent upon him religiously, and spending wealth on what is incumbent upon him is the definition of bribery. In it: He gave to the judge or other something unlawful to arrange a matter, then he arranged it, then regretted — he returns what he gave. So it is apparent that repentance from bribery is by returning the wealth to its owner, even if his need was fulfilled. In Sulh al-Mi'rāj: It is permissible. (In Sulh al-Mi'rāj up to his statement 'the second' — thus found in some copies, and in some copies there is 'kataba' before the following statement 'and not from it is what the woman takes' and it is its place.)
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings on bribery (ar-rishwah) from multiple Hanafi sources. It begins by clarifying the linguistic meaning, noting that the word is with kasr on the bā', and that opening it is colloquial. The difference between a gift (hadiyyah) and a bribe is that a bribe is conditional on receiving help, while a gift has no condition. Then it details four types of bribery from al-Khāniyyah: (1) Bribery that is haram for both giver and taker—this occurs when a person assumes a judgeship through bribery, or gives a bribe to a judge to rule in his favor, whether right or wrong. (2) Bribery given out of fear for life or property is haram for the taker but not the giver; this also applies if the giver fears for his property. (3) Bribery given to arrange one's matter with a ruler: the giver may give but the taker may not take; however, a valid hire contract can be used as a legal stratagem to make it permissible for the taker. If the bribe is given after the matter is arranged without prior condition, it is considered a reward for kindness and is permissible for the taker according to the correct position. (4) What is permissible from both sides is giving gifts for friendship and love, which is not bribery. The passage then provides additional rulings: (a) payments to oppressors for firewood are haram; (b) gifts between lovers are bribery and must be returned; (c) releasing a debtor from debt for arranging a matter is bribery and invalid; (d) a wife releasing her dower for intercourse is valid because it is a gift for friendship; (e) repentance from bribery requires returning the wealth even after the need is fulfilled.

Key Terms
رشوة (rishwah): Payment given with the condition that the recipient provides assistance or favor.
هدية (hadiyyah): Gift given without any condition for favor or help.
مصانعة (muṣāna‘ah): Compensation or payment given to guardians of orphans to secure their cooperation, ruled permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered bribery that is haram for both the giver and the taker?
A) Paying an official to stop an unjust act against you.
B) Giving a judge money to rule in your favor, even if the ruling is correct.
C) Giving a gift to a friend out of love.
D) Hiring a minister for a day to facilitate a contract.

Key Takeaway
Bribery is defined by conditionality; its ruling varies by type, with the giver often excused under duress and the taker prohibited except in cases of merited reward or lawful hire.

Imprisonment for Debt: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute over Badal 'an Mal
Intra-Hanafi dispute on imprisonment for debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on imprisonment for debt, distinguish between badal 'an mal and other debts, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding debts undertaken by contract.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a debtor is imprisoned only for debts that are a substitute for property (badal 'an mal).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles a critical practical question: when can a creditor have a debtor imprisoned? The Hanafi school has an internal difference of opinion on this matter. While the relied-upon position limits imprisonment to debts that are a substitute for property, some scholars extend it to any debt voluntarily undertaken by contract. Understanding this dispute is essential for any judge or mufti dealing with debt collection, as it directly affects the rights of creditors and debtors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing positions
The passage presents two views: (1) the mufta bih (relied-upon) position that imprisonment is only for badal 'an mal (substitute for property), and (2) the minority view that it also covers debts undertaken by contract (iltizam bi 'aqd). Identify the key terms 'badal 'an mal' and 'iltizam bi 'aqd'.
Badal 'an mal includes thaman al-mabi' (price of a sale) and qard (loan). Iltizam bi 'aqd includes mahr (dower) and kafalah (guarantee).
2
Analyze the evidence for each position
The mufta bih relies on the principle that the debtor's ability to pay was established at the time of the substitute, so his later claim of poverty is not trusted. The opposing view argues that any voluntary contractual debt implies prior ability. Note which evidence is considered stronger.
The hanafi_dalil for the mufta bih is: 'al-asl fi al-adami al-'usrah' (original presumption is poverty) but this is overridden for badal 'an mal due to established ability.
3
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the mufta bih, a debtor owing deferred dower or guarantee cannot be imprisoned unless the creditor proves wealth. Under the opposing view, he can be imprisoned on the creditor's mere claim. This difference has real-world implications for family law and commercial guarantees.
The passage explicitly states: 'لا يحبس في المهر والكفالة على المفتى به' (he is not imprisoned for dower and guarantee according to the mufta bih).
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preference) and its authority
The passage notes that Qadi Khan and al-Tarsusi prefer the restriction to badal 'an mal, and that the fatwa is upon it. However, the author of Kanz followed al-Hidayah, which took the broader view. Understand that the mufta bih is based on Abu Hanifa's narration and is considered the zahir al-riwaya.
The authority_weight is 'zahir_riwayah' for the mufta bih, giving it precedence.
5
Apply the rule to the nine excluded cases
The passage lists nine cases that fall under 'other than badal an mal', including substitute for khul', substitute for manumission, maintenance, and arsh. Confirm that in all these cases, the debtor's claim of poverty is accepted unless the creditor proves wealth.
These nine cases are: بدل الخلع، وبدل عتق، وبدل المغصوب، ونفقة الزوجات، ونفقة الأقارب، وأروش الجنايات، العمد وما تأخر من المهر بعد الدخول، وبدل المتلفات.

نقلناه عنهم اهـ . قوله : ( وذكر الطرسوسي (الخ) أعلم أن الطرسوسي نقل عن عدة كتب أن القول للمدعي فيما كان بدل مال لا في غيره كالمهر وبدل الخلع ونقل عن عده كتب أخر أن القول للمدعي فيما كان بدل مال أو التزمه بعقد كالمهر وبدل الكفالة. وعن بعض الكتب القول للمدعي فيما التزمه بعقد باشره لا بما لزمه حكماً بدون مباشرة عقد قال : وهذا يوجب التسوية بين ما كان بدلاً عن مال أو غيره قلت : وأنت خبير بأن الإلتزام بعقد يشمل قولهم ما كان بدلاً عن مال فيكون قولهم والتزمه بعقد من عطف العام الطرسوسي .
Al-Bazzaziyya: The guaranteed person has the power to imprison the guarantor, the principal debtor, and the guarantor of the guarantor, even if numerous, etc. And [this extends] to multiple imprisonments due to multiple claimants: if he was imprisoned for a debt, then another came and claimed the debt against him, he releases him from prison and brings together [the debtor] and the claimant; if he proves his claim, he writes his name and the name of the first; then if another proves, he also writes his name and imprisons him for all of them, and also writes the date; as in al-Bazzaziyya. And he made it absolute, so it indicates that a Muslim is imprisoned for the debt of a dhimmi and a musta'min, and vice versa. And in al-Bazzaziyya: Two men have a debt against a man, one has less and the other more; the one with the lesser [debt] has the right to imprison him, and the one with the greater does not have the right to release him without his consent. And if one of them wants to release him after they both agreed to his imprisonment, he does not have that right. And in al-Qunyah: He was imprisoned for the one with the lesser debt; then the one with the greater debt has the right to release him so that he may earn and pay him, etc. And [it goes] to the point that no one other than his guarantor is imprisoned with the debtor. So if imprisonment of a woman is necessary, she is not imprisoned with her husband, but is imprisoned in her husband's house; as in al-Bazzaziyya. And if a woman imprisons her husband, she is not imprisoned with him; as in al-Khulasa. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: If corruption is feared from her, the later scholars chose to imprison her with him, etc. And in Khizanat al-Fatawa: Some later scholars approved that she be imprisoned with him if [immorality] is feared from her, etc. And in al-Bazzaziyya: Some later scholars approved that the woman be imprisoned if the husband is imprisoned, and Qadi Shah would not imprison her with him to protect her from immorality, etc. And he restricted dower to the prompt [portion] because he is not imprisoned for the deferred [portion], and he is believed regarding inability to pay, and upon this is the fatwa. In al-Asl, he is not believed regarding the dower without distinction between its deferred and prompt portions; as in al-Bazzaziyya. Then know that Qadi Khan in al-Fatawa preferred restriction to the first, and said: Some said: If the debt is obligatory as a substitute for what is property, like a loan and the price of a sale, then the word is that of the claimant of wealth; that is narrated from Abu Hanifa, and upon it is the fatwa, because his ability was established in the substitute, so his statement about the removal of that ability is not accepted. And if the debt is not a substitute for property, then the word is that of the debtor. And some said: Whatever is obligated by his contract, his word is not accepted, even if it is not a substitute for property, etc. So you have learned that the fatwa is upon the first, which is that he is not imprisoned except in what is a substitute for property; so he is not imprisoned for dower and guarantee according to the mufta bih, and that is contrary to the choice of the author following the author of al-Hidayah. And al-Tarsusi in Anfa' al-Wasa'il mentioned that it is the relied-upon school; thus the fatwa has differed regarding what he undertook by contract and was not a substitute for property, and the practice is upon what is in the matn because when what is in the matn and the fatawa conflict, the relied-upon is what is in the matn, as in Anfa' al-Wasa'il. Likewise, what is in the sharh is given precedence over what is in the fatawa. And it was said: The word is the debtor's in everything; and it was said: The word is the creditor's in everything; and it was said: The [multiplicity of] modes is judged except for the fuqaha' and the 'Alawiyya, and al-Zayy as in al-Qamus: with kasra, meaning state, plural azya', etc. And al-Karabisi in al-Furuq corrected it, and in al-Muhit it is the zahir al-riwaya. And by this it is known that what is in al-Mukhtasar is contrary to the zahir al-riwaya and the mufta bih. And he made 'al-madyun' general, so it includes the mukatib, the authorized slave, and the interdicted minor; they are imprisoned, but the minor is not imprisoned for a debt of consumption; rather, his father or guardian is imprisoned. If there is no father or guardian, the judge appoints a man to manage his property for his debt, as in al-Bazzaziyya. His statement: (No) in other than it if he claims poverty, unless his creditor proves his wealth, so he imprisons him as he sees. That is, he does not imprison him in other than what we mentioned that is a substitute for property or undertaken by contract, if he claims poverty, because the origin regarding a person is poverty, and the claimant claims an incidental matter, so it is not accepted from him except with evidence. Under 'other' enter nine cases: substitute for khul', substitute for manumission (the share of the partner), substitute for the usurped object, maintenance of wives, maintenance of relatives, 'Arsh of injuries (intentional and what is delayed of dower after consummation), and substitute for destroyed property. And al-Tarsusi mentioned and erred, and this is the choice of al-Balkhi. His statement: (And al-Tarsusi mentioned that it is the school) where he said: It is established for us from all these transmitted statements that the relied-upon school is that regarding what obliged the debtor as a substitute for property or by a contract that occurred by his choice, the word is that of the claimant, not the debtor, etc. His statement: (And by this it is known that what is in al-Mukhtasar is contrary to the zahir al-riwaya and the mufta bih. Al-Ramli said: As for it being contrary to the zahir al-riwaya, it is due to what is in al-Muhit; and as for it being contrary to the mufta bih, it is due to what is in Qadi Khan. Although Qadi Khan said the fatwa is upon that which is obligated by a contract he directly undertook by his choice, the word is that of the claimant of wealth. Ponder upon what is in al-Mukhtasar against it, the authors of the matn, and he mentioned that it is the relied-upon school. So one could say it is not contrary to the mufta bih, so ponder. His statement: (And substitute for the usurped object) i.e., not the object itself, so it does not contradict what was mentioned from al-Qalanisi. And in al-Minah from Anfa' al-Wasa'il, he placed that in confession of usurpation, i.e., not in what is proven by evidence. Its text: In Anfa' al-Wasa'il, his statement: 'Substitute for the usurped object' means that if he confessed to usurpation and said he is poor, and the person from whom it was usurped said he is wealthy, and they both agree on the destruction, or he was imprisoned due to knowledge of destruction, the word is that of the usurper regarding inability to pay, not the word of the person from whom it was usurped; thus mentioned by al-'Attabi, Taj al-Shari'ah, and Hamid al-Din al-Darir in what we have transmitted from them, etc. His statement: (And al-Tarsusi mentioned, etc.) Know that al-Tarsusi transmitted from several books that the word is that of the claimant in what is a substitute for property, not in other than it like dower and substitute for khul'; and he transmitted from several other books that the word is that of the claimant in what is a substitute for property or what he undertook by contract, like dower and substitute for guarantee. And from some books: the word is that of the claimant in what he undertook by a contract he directly executed, not in what obliged him by law without direct contract. He said: This necessitates equality between what is a substitute for property and other than it. I say: You are aware that undertaking by contract includes their saying 'what is a substitute for property', so their saying 'and he undertook by contract' is an inclusive conjunction. Al-Tarsusi.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, addressing the conditions under which a debtor may be imprisoned. It opens with general rulings from al-Bazzaziyya on multiple guarantors and multiple claimants, establishing that a creditor can imprison both the guarantor and the principal, and that a debtor can be imprisoned for the debts of a dhimmi or musta'min. The passage then moves to the core issue: which debts justify imprisonment and whose word — the creditor's or the debtor's — is accepted regarding the debtor's ability to pay. The author distinguishes between debts that are a substitute for property (badal 'an mal), like the price of a sale or a loan principal, and other debts like dower or guarantee. The relied-upon position (mufta bih) according to the fatwa is that imprisonment is only for the former, and the creditor's claim of the debtor's wealth is accepted, because the debtor had the ability at the time of the substitute. For other debts, the debtor's claim of poverty is accepted unless the creditor proves wealth. However, there is intra-Hanafi disagreement: some scholars extend this to debts undertaken by contract (like dower), arguing that the debtor's voluntary assumption of the debt indicates prior ability. Others restrict it further. The passage also clarifies that a minor is not imprisoned for consumption debts; instead, his father or guardian is held, or a manager is appointed. The text weaves these positions together, citing authorities like Qadi Khan, al-Tarsusi, and al-Balkhi, and concludes that the practice (al-'amal) is upon the matn texts, which give precedence to the statement of the debtor in all cases except badal 'an mal. The commentary highlights the tension between the matn, the fatwa, and the zahir al-riwaya, and notes that the author of Kanz followed al-Hidayah, which is contrary to the accepted fatwa. Ultimately, the student should understand that the Hanafi school limits imprisonment for debt to specific circumstances, with the burden of proof on the creditor in most cases.

Key Terms
بدل عن مال (badal 'an mal): A debt that is a substitute for property, such as the price of a sold item (thaman al-mabi') or a loan principal (qard). The recipient gives up property in exchange for the debt.
الإعسار (al-i'sar): The debtor's claim of being unable to pay (poverty). It is presumed unless the creditor proves the debtor's wealth (al-yasar).
اليسار (al-yasar): Wealth or ability to pay. The creditor may claim the debtor is wealthy to secure imprisonment.
المفتى به (al-mufta bih): The relied-upon legal opinion that is given fatwa according to the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, in which of the following debts can a debtor be imprisoned if the creditor claims the debtor is wealthy?
A) Deferred dower (mahr mu'ajjal)
B) Price of a sale (thaman al-mabi')
C) Guarantee for a loan (kafalah)
D) Maintenance for a wife (nafaqah)

Key Takeaway
Imprisonment for debt in Hanafi law is restricted to debts that are a substitute for property (badal 'an mal); for all other debts, the debtor is presumed poor unless the creditor proves otherwise.

Parent Not Imprisoned for Child's Debt: Ruling and Proof
Explains the ruling and proof for parent's exemption from imprisonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a parent is not imprisoned for a child's debt, explain the qiyas-based proof, and apply the exception for nafaqah.

Core Idea
A parent is never imprisoned for a child's debt — punishment does not pass upward.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a significant exemption in debt imprisonment law. The Hanafi school holds that a parent, including grandparents, cannot be imprisoned for the debt of their child. The reasoning is rooted in a general principle that a parent is not subject to punishment due to a child, analogous to the absence of qisas and hadd. However, there is an exception for nafaqah, and an intra-Hanafi difference on whether land can be sold to recover the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is imprisoning a parent for the debt of their child. The ruling is that it is not permissible (haram) to imprison the parent.
The act of imprisonment is a form of punishment.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas based on the principle that a parent does not deserve punishment due to a child. This is supported by the absence of qisas and hadd between parent and child.
The dalil is aqli (rational analogy).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Imprisonment for debt is a punishment. Since a parent incurs no qisas or hadd for the child, by analogy, they also incur no imprisonment for the child's debt.
The analogy is based on the common illa of punishment.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa does not allow sale of the parent's land for the debt; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahiban) allow it. The fatwa is on the Sahiban's view.
This difference affects how the judge enforces payment.
5
Apply the nuance
If the parent is wealthy and refuses to pay, the judge may sell movable or immovable property (per Sahiban) after giving a three-day grace. The exception is for nafaqah, where imprisonment is allowed.
The rule is one-directional: a child can be imprisoned for a parent's debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا في دين ولده) أي لا يحبس أصل في دين فرعه لأنه لا يستحق العقوبة بسبب ولده ولذا لا قصاص عليه بقتله ولا بقتل مورثه، ولا يحد بقذفه ولا بقذف أمه الميتة بطلبه. وقولهم هنا إنه لا قصاص بقتله يقتضي أن المراد الأصل أباً أو أماً أو جداً لأب أو لأم لتصريحهم في باب الجنايات أن الجد لأم لا قصاص عليه بقتل ولد بنته فكذا لا يحبس بدينه. وفي المحيط : ولا يحبس الأبوان والجدان والجدتان إلا في النفقة لولدهما .اهـ. وظاهر إطلاقهم أنه لا فرق بين الموسر والمعسر ولكن ينبغي أن يتنبه لشيء وهو أنه إذا كان موسراً وامتنع من قضاء دين ولده وقلنا لا يحبس فالقاضي يقضي دينه من ماله إن كان من جنسه وإلا باعه للقضاء كبيعه مال المحبوس الممتنع قضاء دينه، والصحيح عندهما عقاره كمنقوله ولو قال المديون أبيع عرضي وأقضي ديني أجله القاضي ثلاثة ولا يحبسه ولو له عقار يحبسه ليبيعه ويقضي الدين ولو بثمن قليل كما في البزازية. وسيأتي تمامه في الحجر إن شاء الله تعالى فيبيع القاضي مال الأب لقضاء دين ابنه إذا امتنع لأنه لا طريق له إلا البيع وإلا ضاع. وقيد بدين الولد لأن الولد يحبس بدين أصله ويحبس القريب بدين قريبه كما في الخانية،
His statement: '(not in the debt of his child)' meaning: a parent is not imprisoned for the debt of his child because he does not deserve punishment due to his child. Hence there is no qisas upon him for killing him, nor for killing his inheritor, and he is not hadd'd for slandering him, nor for slandering his deceased mother upon his demand. And their statement here that there is no qisas for killing him implies that the intended meaning of 'parent' is father, mother, grandfather (paternal or maternal), because they stated explicitly in the chapter of crimes that the maternal grandfather has no qisas for killing the son of his daughter. So likewise he is not imprisoned for his debt. And in al-Muhit: The parents, grandparents (paternal and maternal) are not imprisoned except for the maintenance of their child. End. And apparent from their absolute statement is that there is no difference between the wealthy and the poor. But one should pay attention to something: if the parent is wealthy and refuses to pay his child's debt, and we say he is not imprisoned, then the judge pays his debt from his property if it is of the same type, otherwise he sells it to pay the debt, just like selling the property of an imprisoned person who refuses to pay his debt. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his land is like his movable property [in being subject to sale]. And if the debtor says 'I will sell my goods and pay my debt', the judge gives him three days and does not imprison him. And if he has land, he imprisons him to force him to sell it and pay the debt, even for a low price, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And its completion will come in the chapter of interdiction, God willing. The judge sells the father's property to pay his son's debt if he refuses, because there is no way except sale, otherwise it would be wasted. And it is conditioned by the debt of the child, because the child is imprisoned for the debt of his parent, and a relative is imprisoned for the debt of his relative, as in al-Khaniyyah.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author explains the matn phrase 'not in the debt of his child' meaning a parent is not imprisoned for the debt of his child. The reasoning is that punishment (including imprisonment for debt) is not due from a parent to his child, analogous to other punishments: a parent does not face qisas for killing his child, nor hadd for slandering him. The term 'parent' includes fathers, mothers, and grandparents (both paternal and maternal) as evidenced by the ruling in jinayat that a maternal grandfather faces no qisas for killing his grandson. Al-Muhit adds that even grandparents are not imprisoned except for nafaqah (maintenance) of their child. The author then addresses a nuance: if the parent is wealthy but refuses to pay, the judge can collect the debt from the parent's property directly, selling it if necessary. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, land can be sold like movable property for this purpose. The judge may also give a three-day grace period or imprison the parent if he has land but refuses to sell. The rule is specific to the parent's debt, because the child can be imprisoned for the parent's debt (reverse situation).

Key Terms
أصل (asl): Parent, including father, mother, grandfather (paternal or maternal).
فرع (far'): Child, descendant (son, daughter, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a parent not imprisoned for the debt of their child according to this passage?
A) Because the parent is always poor.
B) Because punishment is not due from a parent to a child, as seen in qisas and hadd.
C) Because the child is always the one to be imprisoned.
D) Because the judge cannot sell the parent's property.

Key Takeaway
A parent is never imprisoned for a child's debt; this is based on the principle that punishment does not ascend, with an exception for nafaqah and an intra-Hanafi difference on land sale.

Seven (Plus Two) Types Not Imprisoned for Debt
Lists categories of debtors not imprisoned for debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven (plus two) categories of debtors who are exempt from imprisonment for debt in Hanafi fiqh and explain the conditions and exceptions for each.

Core Idea
Imprisonment for debt is waived for parents, masters, slaves, mukatibs, minors, and the 'aqila under specific conditions.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists seven categories of debtors who are exempt from imprisonment. It also adds two more, bringing the total to nine. Understanding these exemptions is crucial for applying debt enforcement correctly in Hanafi fiqh, especially when considering the rights of creditors and the special status of certain debtors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven core categories
List the seven categories from the passage: parent for child's debt, master for authorized slave's debt, slave for master's debt, master for mukatib's debt (with condition), mukatib for kitabah debt, minor for consumption debt, and 'aqila for blood money.
Each category has specific conditions; note them as you go.
2
Examine the conditions for each exemption
For each category, identify the condition that triggers the exemption. For example, the parent is exempt only if the debt is of the child, the master is exempt unless the slave is in debt, etc.
Some exemptions are absolute, others are conditional.
3
Analyze the special ruling for the minor
The minor is not imprisoned for consumption debt unless he has a father/executor who refuses to pay, and even then only for discipline if the minor acted intentionally.
This distinguishes intentional acts from unintentional ones.
4
Understand the father-as-guarantor issue
The passage discusses whether a father can be imprisoned when he is a guarantor (kafil) for his son. The fatwa is that he can, because the imprisonment is for the guarantor's right, not the child's debt directly.
This is a practical application of the parent exemption.
5
Note the two additional categories
At the end, two more categories are added: (8) a debtor known to have absent property, and (9) a debtor known to be wealthy and imprisoned. These are not imprisoned because the judge can seize the property.
This brings the total to nine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كتبنا في الفوائد الفقهية أن من لا يحبس سبعة : الأول الأصل في دين فرعه الثاني المولى في دين عبده المأذون غير المديون وإن مديوناً يحبس لحق الغرماء الثالث العبد لا يحبس بدين ،مولاه، أطلقه الشارح فظاهره ولو كان مديوناً . الرابع المولى لا يحبس بدين مكاتبه إن كان من جنس بدل الكتابة لوقوع المقاصة وإلا يحبس لتوقفها على الرضا الخامس لا يحبس المكاتب بدين الكتابة وإن كان ديناً آخر يحبس به للمولى. ومنهم من منعه لأنه يتمكن من إسقاطه بالتعجيز وصححه في المبسوط عن قامت للمحبوس وهو منكر والبيئة متى قامت للمنكر لا تقبل وقولهم إنه موسر ليس كذلك فيقبل اهـ. وحاصله أنهم لو شهدوا وقالوا إنه يملك العقار الفلاني مثلاً وهو منكر لا تقبل لأنه يقول لا أملك ذلك العقار وهم يشهدون له بأنه يملكه والبيئة متى قامت للمنكر لا تقبل بخلاف ما إذا قالوا إنه موسر لأنهم لم يشهدوا له بملك شيء بعينه فلم تكن شهادة له بل عليه لأجل إدامه الحبس فتقبل تأمل قول المصنف : لا في دين ولده) قال الرملي : وقع الاستفتاء عن حبس الأب المكفول عنه لابنه الابن الكفيل هل للكفيل حبس الأب؟ فرأيت بخط بعض الموالي أنه إذا كان كفيلاً عنه لا يحبس إذا حبس هو ونقله عن القهستاني في الكفالة وقال به يشعر قضاء الخلاصة وكتب تحته لا عبرة بما قاله القهستاني في كتاب الكفالة فطلب مني تحقيق ذلك فقلت ربما أغتر القائل بعدم حبسه بقولهم لا يحبس أصل في دين فرعه متوهما أن الكفيل إذا حبس الأب فقد صدق عليه أنه أصل في دين فرعه ولا يغتر به لأنه إنما حبس لحق الكفيل ولذلك يرجع عليه بما أدى فهو محبوس إذا حبس حبس امتنع من الانفاق عليه. وعليه الفتوى كما في أنفع الوسائل السادس لا يحبس صبي على دين الاستهلاك ولو له مال من عروض وعقار إذا لم يكن له أب ولا وصي والرأي إلى القاضي فيأذن في بيع بعض ماله للإيفاء، وإن كان له أب أو وصي فإنه يحبس إذا امتنع من قضاء دينه من ماله ولا يحبس الصبي إلا بطريق التأديب حتى لا يتجاسر إلى مثله إذا باشر شيئاً من أسباب التعدي قصداً، أما إذا كان خطأ فلا كذا في المبسوط من كتاب الكفالة. وفي المحيط : وللقاضي أن يحبس الصبي التاجر على وجه التأديب لا على وجه العقوبة حتى لا يماطل حقوق العباد فإن الصبي يؤدب لينزجر عن الأفعال الذميمة السابع إذا كان للعاقلة عطاء لا يحبسون في دية وأرش العطاء وإن لم يكن لهم عطاء يحبسون ؛ كذا في البزازية. ويزاد هنا مسألتان قدمناهما لا يحبس المديون إذا علم القاضي أن له مالاً غائباً أو محبوساً موسراً فصارت تسعاً.
And we have written in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah that those who are not imprisoned are seven: first, the parent for the debt of his child; second, the master for the debt of his authorized slave who is not in debt, but if the slave is in debt, he is imprisoned for the right of creditors; third, the slave is not imprisoned for the debt of his master, the commentator made it absolute so apparent is even if he is in debt; fourth, the master is not imprisoned for the debt of his mukatib if it is of the same type as the kitabah because of set-off, otherwise he is imprisoned because set-off depends on consent; fifth, the mukatib is not imprisoned for the debt of kitabah, but if there is another debt he is imprisoned for the sake of the master. Some prevented it because he can drop it by forcing inability, and it was corrected in al-Mabsut. From the one imprisoned who is a denier, and testimony when it is for a denier is not accepted, and their statement that he is wealthy is not like that so it is accepted. Summary: if they testify and say he owns such-and-such land and he denies, it is not accepted because he says I don't own that land and they testify that he owns it, and testimony for a denier is not accepted. Unlike if they say he is wealthy because they did not testify for him ownership of a specific thing, so it is not testimony for him but against him for the continuation of imprisonment, so it is accepted. Ponder the statement of the author: 'not in the debt of his child'. Al-Ramli said: A question was asked about imprisoning the father who is guaranteed for his son - the son is the guarantor. Is it for the guarantor to imprison the father? I saw in the handwriting of some Mawali that if he is a guarantor for him, he is not imprisoned when he is imprisoned, and he transmitted it from al-Qahistany in al-Kafalah, and he said it is indicated by the ruling of al-Khulasa. And written under it: no consideration for what al-Qahistany said in Kitab al-Kafalah. Therefore I was asked for verification, so I said: perhaps the one who says he is not imprisoned is misled by their statement that a parent is not imprisoned for the debt of his child, mistakenly thinking that if the guarantor imprisons the father, it applies to him that it is a parent for the debt of his child. But he should not be misled by it because he is only imprisoned for the right of the guarantor, so he has recourse against him for what he paid, so he is imprisoned. If he refrains from spending on him. And upon it is the fatwa as in Anfa' al-Wasa'il. Sixth, a minor is not imprisoned for a debt of consumption even if he has movable property and land, if he has no father or executor, and the opinion is to the judge, so he gives permission to sell some of his property for payment. And if he has a father or executor, then he is imprisoned if he refuses to pay his debt from his property. And the minor is not imprisoned except by way of discipline so that he does not become bold to do similar [acts] if he commits something of transgression intentionally. But if it is unintentional, then no, as in al-Mabsut from Kitab al-Kafalah. And in al-Muhit: The judge may imprison the minor trader for discipline not for punishment so that he does not delay the rights of people; the minor is disciplined to desist from blameworthy actions. Seventh, if the 'aqila have a stipend, they are not imprisoned for the blood money and wound compensation; but if they have no stipend, they are imprisoned, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And here two more issues we mentioned earlier are added: the debtor is not imprisoned if the judge knows he has absent property or is imprisoned and wealthy, so it becomes nine.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a list of categories of debtors who are not imprisoned for debt. The author enumerates seven types from al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah, then adds two more to make nine. The list includes: (1) a parent for the child's debt; (2) a master for the debt of an authorized slave who is not in debt; (3) a slave for the master's debt; (4) a master for the debt of his mukatib if set-off applies; (5) a mukatib for the kitabah debt itself; (6) a minor for consumption debt unless he acts intentionally; and (7) the 'aqila for blood money if they have stipends. After the list, the author includes a discussion about the father as a guarantor: the issue is whether the guarantor (kafil) can imprison the father. The fatwa is that he can, because the imprisonment is for the guarantor's right, not for the child's debt directly. This is followed by details on the minor and the 'aqila.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla): Master of a slave or mukatib.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has a contract of gradual emancipation (kitabah).
عاقلة (aqila): The tribe/group responsible for paying blood money (diyah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is a minor imprisoned for debt?
A) The minor is never imprisoned for debt.
B) The minor is imprisoned only if he has no guardian.
C) The minor is imprisoned if he has a father or executor and refuses to pay from his property, and he acted intentionally.
D) The minor is imprisoned only if he is a trader.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts specific categories of debtors from imprisonment for debt based on their special status, but conditions apply, and later fatwa clarifies that the parent exemption does not cover guarantee situations.
